Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
Being Pleased with God's Decree of Disbelief
Ash'ari distinguishes between being pleased with God's decree and being pleased with disbelief itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari distinguishes between being pleased with God's decree (as just) and being pleased with disbelief itself.

Core Idea
God's decree is just; the thing decreed may be evil.

In this passage, Ash'ari addresses a crucial question: if God decrees everything, including disbelief, can we say we are pleased with His decree? The answer requires careful precision: we are pleased with the act of decreeing, but not with the disbelief itself. This distinction guards against both fatalism and moral relativism.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and initial answer
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the question is posed and the immediate distinction made.
The answer starts with 'We consent that God decreed disbelief as ugly...'
2
Understand the core distinction
Identify the two senses of 'consent': one regarding the decree as an act of God, and one regarding the content of the decree (disbelief). The passage explains we consent to the former, not the latter.
3
Analyze the analogy of the broken wood
Examine the analogy: the piece of wood is both 'God's proof' and 'broken', but we don't say 'God's proof is broken'. Similarly, we can say we consent to God's decree without consenting to disbelief.
This analogy reinforces the need for careful language.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this distinction matters: it allows affirming God's sovereignty over all things while maintaining moral responsibility and the prohibition against being pleased with evil.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari position on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَفْعَالُ العِبادِ مِن خَلْقِ اللَّه
١٨- مسألة
فإن قال قائل : أفترضَوْنَ بقضاء الله وقدرِهِ الكُفْرَ؟
قيل له : نرضى بأن قضى الله تعالى الكفر قبيحًا، وقدَّرَه فاسدا ، ولا نرضى بأنْ كانَ الكافرُ به كافرًا ؛ لأن الله تعالى نهانا عن ذلك ، وليس إذا أطلقنا الرضا بلفظ القضاء، وجَبَ أن نطلقه بلفظ الكفر، كما لا يجب إذا قلنا : إنَّ الخشبةَ حَجَّةُ اللَّه تعالى ، وإنَّ الخشبة مكسورة، أن نقول : حُجةُ اللهِ تعالى مكسورة ؛ لأن هذا يوهم أن حجة الله تعالى لا حقيقة لها ، فكذلك نُطلِقُ الرضا بلفظ القضاء والقدر، ولا نطلقه بلفظ الكُفْرِ. هذا جواب أصحابنا الذين ذكرنا جوابهم آنفًا
Actions of servants are from Allah's creation.
Issue 18: If someone says: Do you consent to God's decree and measure of disbelief? It is said to him: We consent that God decreed disbelief as ugly (qabihan) and measured it as corrupt (fasidan), but we do not consent that the disbeliever be a disbeliever because of it, since God Almighty has forbidden us from that. And it is not the case that if we use consent (rida) with the word decree, it becomes necessary to use it with the word disbelief. Just as it is not necessary when we say: The piece of wood is God's proof and the piece of wood is broken, that we say: God's proof is broken, because this gives the illusion that God's proof has no reality. So likewise we use consent with the word decree and measure, and we do not use it with the word disbelief. This is the answer of our companions whose answer we mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer (mas'ala) about whether one can be pleased with God's decree of disbelief. The author first states the section heading: 'Actions of servants are from Allah's creation.' Then the questioner asks: 'Do you consent to God's decree and measure of disbelief?' The answer begins by making a crucial distinction: we consent that God decreed disbelief as 'ugly' and 'corrupt', i.e., He decreed it as something bad and flawed. But we do not consent to the disbeliever actually being a disbeliever because of it, since God has forbidden us from being pleased with disbelief (cf. Quran 4:79). The author then explains that just because we use the word 'consent' (rida) in connection with 'decree' does not mean we must use it with 'disbelief'. He gives an analogy already used earlier: if we say that a piece of wood is both God's proof and broken, we do not say 'God's proof is broken' because that would imply God's proof is flawed. Similarly, we can say we are pleased with God's decree (which is just) without being pleased with the disbelief itself. The conclusion is that the answer of 'our companions' is the one already mentioned earlier in the text (referring to the first lesson on cautious phrasing).

قضاء الله
قدر
كفر
قبيح
فاسد
حجة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being pleased with God's decree of disbelief means one must be pleased with disbelief itself.
Correction: The passage explains that one can be pleased with the decree (as just) without being pleased with the content (disbelief), just as one does not call God's proof broken even if the wood is broken.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari answer in this passage, in what sense can one be pleased with God's decree of disbelief?
A) One is pleased that God decreed disbelief as ugly and corrupt.
B) One is pleased that disbelief exists.
C) One is pleased with the disbeliever's disbelief.
D) One is not pleased at all with any aspect of God's decree regarding disbelief.

Key Takeaway
We are pleased with God's decree as an act of justice, but not with the disbelief itself, just as we do not call God's proof broken even if the wood is broken.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a close relative or friend reject faith, do you find yourself questioning God's wisdom in allowing that? How does the distinction in this lesson protect you from being displeased with God's decree while still hating the disbelief itself? Will you commit to separating your view of God's justice from your feelings about the person's choice?
?
Are there sins in your own life that you have resigned yourself to because 'it was decreed'? How does the fact that God decreed them as ugly and corrupt—not as something to be pleased with—change your responsibility to strive against them? What will you do today to repent of that resignation?
?
If you truly believed that God's decree is always just, even when it involves evil, would you still be able to trust Him when you face trials that seem unfair? What area of your life reveals that you might be more upset with the thing decreed than with your own sin? Ask Allah to realign your heart with His wisdom.

Divine Decree

The First Answer: Distinguishing Divine Decree from the Act of Disbelief
Objection and first answer: distinguishing decree from disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Ash'ari response to the objection, which distinguishes between being pleased with the decree itself and being pleased with the act of disbelief.

Core Idea
We accept the decree, not the disbelief; word meanings depend on context.

The objector tries to trap the Ash'ari by claiming that being pleased with Allah's decree logically requires being pleased with disbelief. The first answer clarifies that 'pleasure' applies to the decree as a divine act, not to the immoral act itself. An analogy with a broken piece of wood illustrates the linguistic and theological nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first answer
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the structure: objection followed by the answer's two parts.
Pay attention to the phrase 'نرضى بأن قضى الله تعالى الكفر قبيحًا'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice how the answer separates the decree (qaḍā') from the act (kufr). The term 'pleasure' applies to the decree, not the act.
The passage explicitly says 'ليس إذا أطلقنا الرِّضَا بلفظ القضاء، وجَبَ أن نطلقه بلفظ الكفر'.
3
Understand the analogy
Analyze the analogy of the wooden proof (ḥujjah): just as we don't say 'Allah's proof is broken' when the wood breaks, we don't say we are pleased with disbelief itself.
The analogy shows that a term can be context-sensitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَفْعَالُ العِبادِ مِن خَلْقِ اللَّه
١٨- مسألة
فإن قال قائل : أفترضَوْنَ بقضاء الله وقدرِهِ الكُفْرَ؟
قيل له : نرضى بأن قضى الله تعالى الكفر قبيحًا، وقدَّرَه فاسدا ، ولا نرضى بأنْ كانَ الكافرُ به كافرًا ؛ لأن الله تعالى نهانا ذلك ، وليس إذا أطلقنا الرِّضَا بلفظ القضاء، وجَبَ أن نطلقه بلفظ الكفر، كما لا يجب إذا قلنا : إِنَّ الخشبةَ حَجَّةُ اللَّه تعالى ، وإنَّ الخشبة مكسورة، أن نقول : حُجةُ الله تعالى مكسورة ؛ لأن هذا يُوهِمُ أن حجة الله تعالى لا حقيقة لها ، فكذلك نُطلِقُ الرّضا بلفظ القضاء والقدر، ولا نطلقه بلفظ الكُفْرِ. هذا جواب أصحابنا الذين ذكرنا جوابهم آنفًا
ومن أصحابنا من يجيب : بأن نرضى بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره، اللذين أُمرنا أن نرضى بهما ؛ اتباعًا لأمره. لا نتقدم بين يديه ولا نعترض عليه . وهذا كما نرضى بقاء النبيين عليهم السلام ونكره موتهم ونكره بقاء الشياطين، وكل بقضاء ربِّ العالمين.
Actions of servants are from Allah's creation.
18th question:
If someone says: 'Do you accept with pleasure (tarḍawna) the divine decree and destiny of disbelief?'
It is said to him: 'We accept that Allah—exalted is He—decreed disbelief as ugly (qabīḥan) and destined it as corrupt (fāsidan), but we do not accept that the disbeliever is a disbeliever by it (i.e., by the decree); because Allah—exalted is He—forbade us that. And it is not the case that when we use the term 'pleasure' (al-riḍā) with the word 'decree' (al-qaḍā'), we must necessarily use it with the word 'disbelief'. Just as it is not necessary, if we say: 'The piece of wood is a proof (ḥujjah) of Allah—exalted is He—' and the piece of wood is broken, that we say: 'The proof of Allah—exalted is He—is broken'; because this would suggest that the proof of Allah—exalted is He—has no reality. So likewise, we use the term 'pleasure' with the word 'decree and destiny' (al-qaḍā' wa'l-qadar), and we do not use it with the word 'disbelief.' This is the answer of our companions whose answer we mentioned just now.'
And among our companions there is one who answers: 'That we accept with pleasure the decree of Allah—exalted is He—and His destiny, those two which we have been commanded to accept with pleasure, in obedience to His command. We do not advance before Him nor do we object to Him. And this is like we accept with pleasure the survival (baqā') of the prophets—peace be upon them—and we dislike (nakrah) their death, and we dislike the survival of the devils, and all is by the decree of the Lord of the worlds.'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and two answers concerning whether Ash'aris are pleased (ridā) with divine decree and destiny regarding disbelief. The passage is a question-and-answer format common in kalām works.

First, the objector asks: 'Do you accept with pleasure the divine decree and destiny of disbelief?' The question implies a dilemma: if you accept divine decree, you must also accept disbelief itself, which is unethical.

The first answer (attributed to some companions) begins by clarifying that they accept that Allah decreed disbelief as ugly (qabīḥ) and destined it as corrupt (fāsid). They do not, however, accept that the disbeliever is a disbeliever 'by it' (i.e., by the decree). The reason is that Allah has forbidden them to be pleased with disbelief. The key distinction is between the decree as decree (qaḍā') and the actual act or its attribution. Just because one is pleased with the decree does not automatically mean one is pleased with the labeled act. They use an analogy: if a piece of wood is a proof (ḥujjah) of Allah, and that piece becomes broken, we do not say 'the proof of Allah is broken,' because that would imply the proof has no reality. Similarly, the term 'pleasure' applies to the decree itself, not to the disbelief that is decreed. This answer emphasizes a semantic and theological nuance.

The second answer (from other companions) takes a different approach: they accept with pleasure the decree and destiny because they were commanded to do so, in obedience to Allah's order. They do not advance before Him or object. They illustrate with examples: we are pleased with the survival (baqā') of the prophets but dislike their death; we dislike the survival of the devils—yet all of this is by the decree of the Lord. Thus, being pleased with the decree does not mean being pleased with every particular outcome; rather, it means accepting the divine will while human emotions may differ. This answer focuses on submission and obedience.

Key Terms
قضاء الله
قدر
الرضا
الكفر
حجة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris are pleased with disbelief itself because they believe everything is decreed by Allah.
Correction: They are pleased only with the divine decree as an act of Allah, not with the evil act of disbelief itself.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, why do Ash'aris not say they are pleased with disbelief?
A) Because disbelief is not decreed by Allah.
B) Because being pleased with the decree does not entail being pleased with the act named by the decree.
C) Because Allah forbids being pleased with disbelief.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
In the first answer, Ash'aris affirm pleasure with the divine decree but not with the evil act itself, using linguistic precision to avoid contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems genuinely evil—like injustice or suffering—do you find it easy to accept Allah’s decree while still hating the evil? Or does your heart become restless? This lesson gives a distinction: you can submit to the decree while rejecting the evil. Where in your life today can you practice this distinction?
?
Are there sins you have committed that you now think, 'It was written for me, so it’s okay'? How does this lesson’s teaching that we are pleased with the decree (that it is ugly) but not with the act itself challenge that excuse? Will you still use divine decree as a cover for your choices?
?
The analogy of the wood shows that words change meaning based on context. Is your relationship with Allah’s decree based on the actual words of revelation and sound reasoning, or have you let your desires twist the meanings? Pledge to seek the truth even when it demands you reject what you want.

Divine Decree

The Second Answer: Submission to Divine Decree as Command
Second answer: obedience-based acceptance of divine decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Ash'ari answer, which bases pleasure in divine decree on obedience to Allah's command and allows natural human dislike for certain outcomes.

Core Idea
We accept the decree because Allah commanded, not because we like everything decreed.

The second answer takes a more direct approach: Ash'aris are pleased with the decree because they are commanded to be, but that does not mean they are emotionally pleased with every outcome. Examples of liking the prophets' survival but disliking their death—and disliking the devils' survival—illustrate that one can accept the decree while having personal preferences.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer statement
Read the Arabic and translation of this short passage, noting the reason given for being pleased: because we are commanded.
The phrase 'اللذين أُمرنا أن نرضى بهما' is key.
2
Understand the principle of obedience
The answer emphasizes submission: we do not advance before Allah or object to Him. Pleasure is an act of worship.
Contrast with the first answer which used linguistic distinction.
3
Reflect on the examples
The examples of prophets and devils show that we can have different emotional responses to different decrees, yet all are accepted as from Allah.
We like the prophets' survival but dislike their death; we dislike the devils' survival. All are by decree.

D) Because they have no choice.

Key Takeaway
The second answer bases pleasure in divine decree on obedience to Allah's command, allowing natural human dislike for evil outcomes while affirming the decree's goodness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a specific event in your life that you have outwardly accepted but inwardly resent? Can you honestly say you are pleased with it because Allah commanded, or do you still hold a grudge against the decree?
?
You claim to accept divine decree, but when a tragedy occurs, do your complaints reveal that you are actually objecting to Allah's command? How can you align your heart with obedience even when your emotions rebel?
?
The passage uses the example of liking prophets' survival but disliking their death. Think of a time you disliked something that happened to a righteous person. Did that dislike lead you to question Allah's decree, or did you submit despite the pain? What would it take for you to reach the level of 'we do not object'?

Being Pleased with Divine Decree: Submission Without Approval of Evil
Al-Ash'ari explains the stance on being pleased with divine decree despite disliking certain events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari scholars reconcile being pleased with divine decree while still disliking evil events.

Core Idea
Being pleased with Allah's decree means accepting His will, not approving of everything that happens.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari presents a nuanced answer to an implied objection: how can believers be pleased with all of Allah's decree when some events are evil? He explains that our pleasure is obedience to the command, not an emotional approval of every outcome. An analogy with prophets and devils clarifies this subtle point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of classical kalam.
2
Identify the core distinction
The passage distinguishes between being pleased with the decree as an act of obedience and naturally disliking certain events. Find where this distinction is made.
The key is 'اتباعًا لأمره' (in obedience to His command).
3
Analyze the analogy
The analogy involves prophets and devils. Why does the author choose these examples? What does it teach about pleasure and dislike?
Both survival of prophets and devils are decreed, but we have opposite feelings towards them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a response to an implied objection about being pleased with all divine decrees, including evil. The author cites some of his companions' answer: they affirm being pleased with qada and qadr because Allah commanded it. They clarify that this does not mean they approve of everything; rather, they follow the command. They give an analogy: they are pleased with the prophets' continued existence and dislike their death, yet both are by divine decree. Similarly, they dislike the survival of devils. This shows that being pleased with the decree, in the sense of accepting Allah's will, does not negate natural human emotions of dislike for certain outcomes, as long as one does not object to Allah's wisdom. This is a nuanced Ash'ari position that balances submission to divine will with human emotional responses.

Attributing Evil to Allah: General vs. Specific and Ash‛ari's View
Ashari explains the stance on attributing evil to Allah and gives his own qualified view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced Ash‛ari position on whether evil can be attributed to Allah, distinguishing between general and specific attribution and the qualification 'evil for others, not for Him.'

Core Idea
Evil is from Allah only as created harm for creatures, not as an attribute of the Creator.

This passage from Al-Luma‘ tackles a sensitive theological question: do we say that evil comes from Allah? The answer is not a simple yes or no. We first learn the cautious approach of some Ash‛ari scholars who only affirm all things from Allah in general, avoiding explicit mention of evil. Then Imam Ash‛ari himself gives a more precise formulation that upholds divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the literal sense. Notice the question and the two responses.
The passage uses analogies to clarify the difference between general and specific attribution.
2
Analyze the first view: general vs. specific
Identify the view of 'some of our companions.' They say all things are from Allah in general, but do not explicitly say 'evil is from Allah' in detail. Understand the analogy with wife/child and weakness.
This view is cautious to avoid saying something that could be misunderstood.
3
Examine Ash‛ari's own view
Focus on Ash‛ari's statement: evil is from Allah 'in that He created it as evil for others, not for Himself.' This is the key qualification.
This preserves divine transcendence and justice.
4
Identify the misconception and its correction
The passage guards against the misconception that attributing evil to Allah implies injustice or imperfection in Allah. The correction is the two-words qualification 'for others, not for Him.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللُّمَع في الردّ على أَهْلِ الزَّيْغِ والبِدَع
-۲۰- مسألة
[في خَلْق الخيرِ والشّرّ]
فإنْ قالَ : أَوَتقولون : إنَّ الشَّرَّ مِنَ الله -تعالى-؟
قيل :له من أصحابنا من يقول : بأنَّ الأشياء كلَّها الجملة، ولا يطلق بلفظ الشر أنه الله -تعالى- كما يقال : من الأشياء كلها لله في الجملة، ولا يقال على التفصيل : الزوجة ولا الولد لله -تعالى-، وكما نقول في الجملة : ما دون الله ضعيف، ولا يقال على التفصل : دين الله ضعیف
(قال الشيخ أبو الحسن -رحمه الله -: فأمَّا أَنَا فَإِنِّي أقولُ : إِنَّ الشَّرَّ من الله -تعالى- بأن خلقه شرا لغيره لا له)
Al-Luma‘ in refutation of the people of deviation and innovation. 20th Issue. On the creation of good and evil. If he says: Do you say that evil is from Allah - exalted? It is said to him: Some of our companions say that all things are from Allah in general (jumla), and they do not explicitly say that evil is from Allah - exalted - just as it is said: all things are from Allah in general, and it is not said in detail: the wife and the child are from Allah - exalted - and just as we say in general: what is other than Allah is weak, and it is not said in detail: the religion of Allah is weak. (Shaykh Abu al-Hasan - may Allah have mercy on him - said: As for me, I say that evil is from Allah - exalted - in that He created it as evil for others, not for Himself.)
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage addresses the theological issue of whether evil is attributable to Allah. It begins with a question: do you affirm that evil is from Allah? The initial response reports the view of some of Ash‘ari's companions: they say that all things are from Allah in a general sense (fī l-jumla), but they do not explicitly say that evil is from Allah (lā yuṭlaqu bi-lafẓi l-sharri annahu min Allāh). They draw analogies: just as we say all things are from Allah in general but do not say in detail that a wife or child is from Allah (because these are specific created things that are not attributed directly to Allah in that manner), and just as we say in general that everything besides Allah is weak but do not say that the religion of Allah is weak. This cautious position avoids directly attributing evil to Allah in a specific statement. Then the author, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash‘ari, gives his own view: he says that evil is from Allah, but with a crucial qualification: 'in that He created it as evil for others, not for Himself' (bi-anna khalaqahu sharran li-ghayrihi lā lahu). That is, Allah creates evil as a quality that harms creatures, but it is not evil in relation to Allah because He is not subject to harm and His actions are just. Thus, Ash‘ari affirms that evil is created by Allah but without any imperfection or injustice attributed to the Creator.

Key Terms
شر
خلق
جملة
تفصيل
جور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we say evil is from Allah, then Allah is unjust or does evil.
Correction: Ash‛ari clarifies that evil is from Allah only as a creation that is evil for others, not for Allah Himself. Thus Allah's actions remain just and free of imperfection.

Checkpoint
According to Ash‛ari's own view in this passage, in what sense is evil from Allah?
A) Evil is from Allah in the same way as good, without distinction.
B) Evil is from Allah because He creates it as evil for others, not for Himself.
C) Evil is not from Allah at all; it is from human actions alone.
D) Evil is from Allah only in general, not in detail.

Key Takeaway
Evil is from Allah only in the sense that He creates it as a harm for creatures, not as an attribute of His own essence or action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience or witness something you consider evil, does your heart automatically question Allah's goodness? How can Ash'ari's careful wording—'evil for others, not for Himself'—reshape your trust in His justice right in that moment?
?
The companions avoided even the general phrase 'evil is from Allah' because of the risk of misunderstanding. Are there careless words you use about Allah's actions—in complaint or in explanation—that subtly poison your own faith or others'? What will you guard your tongue from starting today?
?
If someone suffering from a tragedy asked you, 'Why did Allah create this evil?' would you be able to answer without either accusing Allah or denying His control? This lesson gives you both truth and caution. Are you ready to hold that tension in your own soul before you speak to theirs?

Attribution of Evil to Allah
Al-Ash'ari addresses whether evil is from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians reconcile the attribution of evil to Allah without attributing evil to His essence, through the distinction between general and specific attribution and the concept of creation as evil for others.

Core Idea
Evil is from Allah as created for others, not as an attribute of His essence.

This lesson addresses a sensitive theological question: if Allah creates everything, does that include evil? The objector challenges the Ash'ari position, and the response reveals a sophisticated distinction between speaking in general terms (jimala) and in detail (tafsil). Al-Ash'ari himself provides a precise formulation that safeguards divine transcendence while affirming Allah's absolute creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and responses
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the structure: an objection, then a first answer, then al-Ash'ari's own answer.
The first answer is attributed to 'some of our companions'.
2
Understand the jimala/tafsīl distinction
Notice that the first answer avoids saying 'evil is from Allah' in detail, using analogies like not saying 'the wife belongs to Allah' specifically.
This preserves reverence while affirming general ownership.
3
Grasp al-Ash'ari's precise formulation
Al-Ash'ari states clearly that evil is from Allah in that He created it as evil for others, not for Himself. This is the key doctrinal point.
It avoids attributing evil to Allah's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: أوتقولون: إن الشر من الله تعالى؟ قيل له: من أصحابنا من يقول بأن الأشياء كلها لله في الجملة، ولا يطلق بلفظ الشر أنه من الله تعالى كما يقال: من الأشياء كلها لله في الجملة، ولا يقال على التفصيل: الزوجة ولا الولد لله تعالى، وكما نقول في الجملة: ما دون الله ضعيف، ولا يقال على التفصيل: دين الله ضعيف. قال الشيخ أبو الحسن رحمه الله: فأما أنا فإني أقول: إن الشر من الله تعالى بأن خلقه شرا لغيره لا له.

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector asks whether Ash'aris say that evil comes from Allah. Second, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari himself states that evil is from Allah in the sense that He creates it as evil for others, not that it is evil for Allah Himself. This safeguards divine transcendence while affirming Allah's creation of all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If evil were truly from Allah in the same way goodness is, would you worship Him with the same love? Now that you know He only creates evil for others, not as His own deficiency, does your love become purer or more conflicted? Be honest – and then act on that honesty by trusting His wisdom in the trials He sends.

The Inconsistency of Denying God's Creation of Disobedience
Refutation of Qadariyya using reductio and Qur'anic argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari refutes the Qadariyya by showing that their own criteria for creation are inconsistent when applied to both obedience and disobedience, and how Qur'anic verses affirm the universality of God's creation.

Core Idea
If diversity prevents creation, then obedience must be created—a self-defeating claim.

This passage presents Imam al-Ash'ari's logical refutation of the Qadariyya who argued that acts of disobedience are not created by God because they are diverse. By turning their own argument against them, the Imam exposes the inconsistency in their reasoning. He then reinforces the argument with Qur'anic verses that affirm God's creation of all things, addressing a potential objection about the phrase 'created with truth.'

Guided Path
1
Read and follow the reductio ad absurdum
The passage begins by taking the opponent's claim that diversity prevents creation. Apply this logic to acts of obedience, which are not diverse and which the opponent admits are from God. Notice the logical inconsistency.
This step reveals the flaw in the opponent's reasoning.
2
Examine the argument from Qur'anic universality
The author then cites verse 'He created everything' (al-An'am: 102) and argues that it is general, covering all things other than God. Consider the implication for acts of disobedience.
The verse 'Ahsana kulla shay'in khalaqahu' (Sajda: 7) is also used to support the generality.
3
Understand the objection and its answer
The opponent raises the verse 'We did not create the heavens and the earth except in truth' (Ahqaf: 3) to suggest a restriction. The answer clarifies that 'truth' refers to God's command 'Be', which itself is an act of creation, thus maintaining the universality.
This shows that the verse does not limit God's creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يخلقه الله فلم لا تكونُ الطاعات مخلوقة له؛ لأنها عندكم من عند الله تعالى؟! وإن كان الكفر والمعاصي غير مخلوقة لله تعالى؛ لأنها متفاوتة، فلم لا تكون الطاعات مخلوقة له؛ لأنها عندكم غير متفاوتة؟! وإذا كان قوله سبحانه: ﴿أَحْسَنَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقَهُ﴾ [السجدة: ٧] على العموم في كل شيء خلقه الله تعالى، فلم لا كان قوله تعالى: ﴿خَلَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٠٢] على العموم في كل شيء هو غيره؟ فإن قال: فما معنى قوله: ﴿مَا خَلَقْنَا السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا إِلَّا بِالْحَقِّ﴾ [الأحقاف: ٣]؟ قيل له: خلق الله ذلك بأن قال له: كن، فالحق: قوله لهما: كوناً، فكانتا.
Did God not create it, so why are acts of obedience not created for Him, because according to you they are from God Almighty?! And if disbelief and sins are not created by God Almighty because they are diverse, then why are acts of obedience not created for Him, because according to you they are not diverse?! And if His statement, Exalted is He: 'Who made beautiful everything He created' [Sajda: 7] is general in every thing that God Almighty created, then why was His statement: 'He created everything' [An'am: 102] not general in every thing other than Him? But if he says: What is the meaning of His statement: 'We did not create the heavens and the earth and what is between them except in truth' [Ahqaf: 3]? It is said to him: God created that by saying to it: 'Be', so the truth is His statement to them: 'Be', and they were.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a series of arguments refuting the Qadariyya claim that some acts (especially sins) are not created by God. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the opponent argues that disbelief and sins are not created by God because they are varied (متفاوتة), then by the same logic, acts of obedience (which the opponent admits are from God and are not varied) should be created by God. But that is consistent with the opponent's view, so the argument is actually to show the inconsistency in the opponent's reasoning about disobedience. Then the author uses the verse 'He created everything' to argue that all things other than God are created by Him. An objection is raised about the verse 'created the heavens and earth with truth' which might imply a restriction. The answer is that the 'truth' here refers to God's command 'Be', which in itself is an act of creation, so the verse does not limit the universality of creation.

Key Terms
الطاعات
المعاصي
مخلوقة
متفاوتة
العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qadariyya believed that diversity (تفاوت) of actions (like sins) proves they are not created by God.
Correction: The same reasoning would force them to admit that uniform acts of obedience (which are from God) are also created by God, leading to inconsistency. In fact, both obedience and disobedience are created by God as shown by the general verses.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main point in the first part of this passage?
A) To prove that acts of obedience are not created by God.
B) To show that the opponent's criterion for creation (diversity) is inconsistent when applied to both obedience and disobedience.
C) To argue that God did not create the heavens and the earth with truth.
D) To establish that the verse 'He created everything' is not general.

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya claim that diversity prevents creation is self-defeating because it would require them to admit that uniform acts of obedience are created by God, which they already affirm come from Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you catch yourself subconsciously separating that action from God's decree, as if it happened outside His will? How does this passage challenge you to accept that even your worst moments are under His creation, and what does that mean for your repentance?
?
The Qadariyya wanted to 'protect' God from evil, but ended up denying His complete sovereignty. Are there areas of your life where you try to reserve control for yourself, thinking that God's involvement would make things imperfect? Would you be willing to surrender that last stronghold and say, 'He is the Creator of everything'?
?
Read the translation again: 'God created that by saying to it: Be, so the truth is His statement to them: Be, and they were.' Does your tongue reflect this truth when you face difficulty, or do you complain as if something happened without His command? What one habit can you change today to live in constant awareness that every event—whether pleasing or painful—is enacted by His word 'Be'?

Proof from Divine Attributes That All Actions Are Created by God
New question (25) arguing from God's knowledge, power, and creation to the creation of all actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari argument uses God's universal knowledge, power, and creation to demonstrate that all human actions are created by God.

Core Idea
The scope of God's attributes is universal—His knowledge, power, and creation encompass everything.

This passage presents a powerful argument directed at the Qadariyya, who claimed that humans create their own actions. By drawing an analogy from God's all-encompassing knowledge to His power and creation, the author forces the opponent to accept that every event—including human actions—falls under God's power and creation. The argument relies on verses the Qadariyya already accept, exposing a logical inconsistency in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that the author uses a rhetorical question to engage the opponent.
Observe the pattern: the author first secures agreement on a universally accepted attribute (knowledge).
2
Identify the three divine attributes
List the three attributes mentioned: knowledge, power, and creation. For each, note the Quranic verse cited.
The attributes are presented in a specific order: knowledge, then power, then creation.
3
Understand the analogy
The author argues that if God's knowledge is universal (covering all known things), then logically His power and creation must also be universal. This forces the Qadariyya to accept that everything, including human actions, is within God's power and created by Him.
The Qadariyya accept the verse about knowledge but inconsistently reject the same scope for power and creation.
4
Reflect on the opponent's inconsistency
Consider why the Qadariyya would accept God's universal knowledge but deny His universal creation. The passage exposes this as a logical contradiction.
This argument is a classic example of iltizam (forcing acceptance of consequences).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لأهل القدر: أليس قول الله تعالى: ﴿بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٩] يدل على أنه لا معلوم إلا والله به عالم؟ فإذا قالوا: نعم. قيل لهم: فما أنكرتم أن يدل قوله تعالى: ﴿عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٠] على أنه لا مقدور إلا والله عليه قادر، وأن يدل قوله تعالى: ﴿خَلَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٠٢] على أنه لا محدث مفعول إلا والله محدث له فاعل خالق؟
And it is said to the people of Qadar: Does not the saying of God Almighty: 'He is of all things Knowing' [Baqara: 29] indicate that there is no known thing except that God is knowledgeable of it? If they say: Yes. It is said to them: Then why do you deny that His saying: 'He is over all things Powerful' [Baqara: 20] indicates that there is no possible thing except that God is powerful over it, and that His saying: 'He created everything' [An'am: 102] indicates that there is no created made thing except that God is its originator, doer, creator?
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy (qiyas) directed at the Qadariyya, who denied that God creates human actions. The author first secures agreement that God's knowledge is universal: the verse 'He is of all things Knowing' implies that everything known is known by God. Then he argues that the same universal scope must apply to God's power (verse 2:20) and His creation (verse 6:102). Therefore, every event—including human actions—is within God's power and created by Him. The argument is structured as a rhetorical question that forces the opponent to accept the logical implication of the verses they already believe in.

Correction: By analogy, if God's knowledge is all-encompassing, then His power and creation must also be all-encompassing. Therefore, human actions are necessarily created by God.

Argument from Divine Attributes against the Qadariyya
Al-Ash'ari argues that divine knowledge, power, and creation encompass all acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses the universally accepted divine attributes of knowledge, power, and creation to argue that all human actions are created by Allah, and why this refutes the Qadariyya position.

Core Idea
Divine attributes prove that everything, including human actions, is under Allah's knowledge, power, and creation.

Al-Ash'ari engages the Qadariyya on their own ground. He begins by citing a verse about divine knowledge that they already accept, then extends the same logic to divine power and creation. This clever dialectical move forces the opponent to either accept the conclusion or reject their own premise. Read the passage to see how the argument unfolds step by step.

Guided Path
1
Set the stage: know the opponent
The Qadariyya deny that Allah creates human actions. Al-Ash'ari will not argue from scratch; he uses what they already accept. Read the passage to see which divine attributes he picks.
The Qadariyya are not atheists; they believe in God but claim humans independently create their own deeds.
2
Analyze the first premise about knowledge
Al-Ash'ari asks whether the verse 'With everything He is All-Knowing' means Allah knows everything. The Qadariyya must say yes. This becomes the foundation of the argument.
Notice the verse reference: Al-Baqarah 2:29.
3
Extend the same logic to power and creation
If the Qadariyya accept the premise for knowledge, they should also accept the parallel premises for power and creation. Read how Al-Ash'ari phrases the extension using the same structure.
The verses cited are Al-Baqarah 2:20 and Al-An'am 6:102.
4
Draw the inevitable conclusion
Since human actions are originated things, and 'Creator of everything' includes them, Allah must be the creator of all actions. The Qadariyya cannot consistently deny this.
This conclusion directly refutes the Qadariyya's core claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَفْعَالُ العِبادِ مِن خَلْقِ الله

ويقال لأهل القدر: أليس قولُ الله -تعالى-: ﴿ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عليم ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٩] يدل على أنه لا معلوم إِلَّا والله به عالمٌ .

So if they say: Yes. It is said to them: What prevents you from [acknowledging] that His saying - most high - 'Over everything All-Powerful' [Al-Baqarah 2:20] indicates that there is no thing possible except that Allah is powerful over it, and that His saying - most high - 'Creator of everything' [Al-An'am 6:102] indicates that there is no originated, made thing except that Allah is its originator, maker, creator?
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the Qadariyya, who claim that humans independently create their own actions. The author presents a dialectical proof based on three divine attributes that the Qadariyya already accept: knowledge, power, and creation. He first asks whether the verse 'With everything He is All-Knowing' implies that everything known is known by Allah. The Qadariyya must answer yes. Then he parallels this reasoning: just as the verse on knowledge shows universal divine knowledge, the verse on power ('Over everything All-Powerful') shows that every possible thing is under His power, and the verse on creation ('Creator of everything') shows that every originated thing is created by Him. Since human actions are originated things, they must be created by Allah. The argument is structured as a syllogism: if the opponent concedes the premise (divine knowledge encompasses all), they must logically concede the conclusion for power and creation as well.

Allah's Disavowal and the Creation of Shirk
Al-Ash'ari refutes the argument that Allah's disavowal of polytheists implies He did not create their shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's logical refutation of the claim that Allah's disavowal of polytheists implies He did not create their polytheism.

Core Idea
Disavowal of persons does not negate divine creation of their actions.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari tackles a subtle argument from those who deny Allah's creation of human actions. They claimed that because Allah says He is disassociated from polytheists, He cannot be the creator of their polytheism. Al-Ash'ari exposes the flaw in this reasoning and turns it against them. Let's work through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Notice how Al-Ash'ari presents the opponent's argument before refuting it.
Pay attention to the Qur'anic verses he quotes.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that Allah's disavowal (bara'ah) of polytheists shows He did not create their shirk. Underline this reasoning in your mind.
This is a key point of contention in qadar discussions.
3
Follow Al-Ash'ari's first refutation
He notes that the verse says 'polytheists' (persons) not 'polytheism' (act). If disavowal of the person meant no creation of the act, it would also mean no creation of the person—which is absurd.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Analyze the second refutation
Al-Ash'ari then turns the argument against the Qadariyya: they accept that Allah is the friend (wali) of believers, yet deny He creates their faith. Inconsistency!
This shows the opponent's logic is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم استثنى قوما من المشركين يقال: إنهم من بني كنانة فقال: ﴿إِلَّا الَّذِينَ عَاهَدتُّمْ عِندَ الْمَسْجِدِ الْحَرَامِ فَمَا اسْتَقَامُوا لَكُمْ فَاسْتَقِيمُوا لَهُمْ﴾ [التوبة: ٧] إلى انقضاء مدتهم. على أن الله -تعالى- ذكر المشركين ولم يقل: من شركهم، ولو كان قوله: ﴿بَرِيءٌ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ﴾ يدل على أنه لم يخلق شركهم لدل على أنه لم يخلقهم؛ لأنه -تعالى- بريء من المشركين، ومن شركهم، ولو كان قوله تعالى: ﴿بَرِيءٌ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ﴾ يوجب أنه ما خلق شركهم؛ للزم القدريَّة؛ إذ قال إنه ولي المؤمنين ﴿وَاللَّهُ وَلِيُّ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ﴾ [آل عمران: ٦٨] فقد خلق إيمانهم، فلما لم يكن هذا -عندهم- هكذا بطل ما قالوه.
Then He excepted a group of the polytheists—it is said they are from Banu Kinanah—and said: 'Except those with whom you have made a treaty near the Sacred Mosque; so as long as they are straight with you, be straight with them' [At-Tawbah 9:7] until the end of their term. Moreover, Allah—Exalted is He—mentioned the polytheists and did not say 'from their polytheism.' If His statement 'I am disassociated from the polytheists' indicated that He did not create their polytheism, it would indicate that He did not create them, because He—Exalted is He—is disassociated from the polytheists and from their polytheism. And if His statement—Exalted is He—'I am disassociated from the polytheists' necessitated that He did not create their polytheism, then it would be necessary for the Qadariyyah, since He said that He is the friend of the believers, 'And Allah is the friend of the believers' [Aal 'Imran 3:68], He indeed created their faith. Since this is not the case according to them, what they said is invalid.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a logical refutation of an argument made by the Qadariyya or others who claimed that Allah's disavowal of the polytheists (as in the verse 'I am disassociated from the polytheists') proves that He did not create their polytheism. Al-Ash'ari begins by noting that the verse about treaties (At-Tawbah 9:7) mentions the polytheists themselves, not their act of polytheism. He then argues: if the disavowal of the polytheists implied that Allah did not create their polytheism, then it would also imply that He did not create them (since He disavows them as persons). But that is false. He then turns the argument against the Qadariyya: they hold that Allah is the friend of the believers (Aal 'Imran 3:68), yet they do not accept that this implies He created their faith—because they deny His creation of actions. So their logic is inconsistent: if disavowal disproves creation, then friendship would prove creation; but they reject the latter, hence their premise is invalid.

Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that disavowal of persons does not entail non-creation of their actions; otherwise it would absurdly imply Allah did not create the polytheists themselves.

The Qadari Accusation Refuted: Affirming Decree Does Not Make You Qadari
Al-Ash'ari refutes the claim that affirming divine decree makes one a Qadari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari refutes the claim that affirming Allah's decree over human actions makes one a Qadari.

Core Idea
Attributing decree to Allah is not the same as being a Qadari.

In this passage, Imam Al-Ash'ari defends the orthodox Sunni position on divine decree against a specific accusation. The Qadariyya sect, who denied predestination, accused those who affirm it of being Qadari themselves. Al-Ash'ari responds with a clever symmetrical argument that exposes the inconsistency of their accusation. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the flow: a statement of position, an analogy, a symmetrical argument, and a conclusion.
The argument uses conditional reasoning, so pay attention to 'if... then' statements.
2
Identify the structure of the refutation
Break down the passage: first, Al-Ash'ari states the orthodox belief. Then he gives an analogy about craftsmanship. Next, he presents two conditional statements that turn the accusation back on the accusers. Finally, he concludes that their claim is invalid.
3
Analyze the symmetrical argument
Focus on the two 'if we were Qadari... then they would be Qadari' statements. This shows that if the accusation were consistent, the accusers themselves would fall under the label. Since they reject that, their charge is baseless.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this refutation is important in Islamic theology: it defends the belief in divine decree (qadar) without falling into fatalism or Qadariyya denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : نحنُ نُثبتُ أنَّ اللَّهَ -تعالى- قدَّرَ أعمالنا وخلَقَها : مقدرة لنا، ولا نُثبت ذلك لأنفسنا. فمَنْ أثبت القدر لله -تعالى-، وزَعَمَ أنَّ الأفعال مُقدَّرةٌ لربه لا يكونُ قدريَّا ، كما أنَّ مَنْ أثبت الصياغة والنجارة لغيرِه لا يكونُ صائِعًا ولا نجارًا . ولو كُنَّا قدريةٌ بقَوْلِنا : إنَّ اللَّهَ فعلَ أفعالَنَا مُقدَّرةً [لَهُ] ، لكانوا قدرية بقولهم : إنَّ الله -تعالى- فعل أفعاله كلها مقدرة له . ولو كُنَّا بقولِنَا : إِنَّ اللَّهَ قدَّرَ المعاصي، قدرية، لكانوا بقولهم : إنَّ اللَّهَ قدَّرَ الطاعاتِ قدرية . فلما لم يكن ذلك كذلكَ بَطَلَ ما قالوه .
It was said to them: We affirm that Allah - exalted is He - decreed our actions and created them: as decreed for us, and we do not affirm that for ourselves. So whoever affirms decree for Allah - exalted is He - and claims that actions are decreed for their Lord is not a Qadari, just as whoever affirms craftsmanship and carpentry for someone else is not a craftsman or a carpenter. And if we were Qadari by our statement: that Allah did our actions as decreed for Him, then they would be Qadari by their statement: that Allah - exalted is He - did all His actions as decreed for Him. And if we were, by our statement: that Allah decreed sins, Qadari, then they would be, by their statement: that Allah decreed acts of obedience, Qadari. Since that is not the case, what they said is invalidated.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an accusation leveled against the author's theological school. The opponents claim that affirming that Allah decrees and creates human actions makes one a Qadari (a member of the Qadariyya sect who denied divine predestination). The author begins by stating the position: 'We affirm that Allah decreed our actions and created them as decreed for us, and we do not affirm that for ourselves.' This establishes that the decree belongs to Allah, not to humans. Then he argues using an analogy: just as attributing craftsmanship to another person does not make you a craftsman, so attributing decree to Allah does not make you a Qadari. The core of the refutation is a symmetrical argument: if their accusers' logic were consistent, then the accusers themselves would be Qadari because they affirm that Allah decrees His own actions and decrees acts of obedience. Since the accusers do not accept that label, their accusation is inconsistent and therefore invalid. The passage concludes: 'Since that is not the case, what they said is invalidated.'

Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that attributing decree to Allah is not equivalent to being a Qadari; rather, the Qadariyya are those who deny divine decree. The accusation is refuted by a symmetry argument: if the logic held, the accusers would themselves be Qadari.

Proof that Human Capacity is an Accident Distinct from the Person
Al-Ash'ari proves that capacity is an accident distinct from the person and begins to argue it cannot precede the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's argument that human capacity (istita'a) is an accident distinct from the person, based on the alternation between capability and incapability, and the beginning of his reasoning that capacity cannot precede the act.

Core Idea
Human capacity is not a permanent faculty but a momentary accident that alternates with incapacity.

Al-Ash'ari opens the discussion on human capacity by addressing a key objection: why is capacity something other than the person himself? He provides a compelling analogy with knowledge and motion, showing that since a person alternates between being capable and incapable, capacity must be a separate, changeable attribute. This lesson unpacks that proof and then turns to the question of whether capacity can exist before the act, a topic the text begins to explore.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and the analogy
The objector asks why capacity is other than the person. Al-Ash'ari uses the alternation between capability and incapability, similar to knowledge and motion, to argue that capacity must be a distinct accident.
Notice how al-Ash'ari draws on observable changes in the human state.
2
Follow the logical proof
If capacity were identical to the person or an inseparable attribute, the person would always be capable. But since we observe both capability and incapability, capacity cannot be the person himself or a permanent attribute; it must be a separate accident.
This is an argument from alternation (tawārud al-ḥāl).
3
Introduce the second objection
After establishing capacity as an accident, the next objection questions whether this capacity can precede the act. The text begins to answer that the act must occur with or after the capacity, implying simultaneity or immediate conjunction.
The passage is cut off here, but the initial premise is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الاستطاعة

إن قال قائل : لمَ قلتم : إن الإنسان يستطيع باستطاعة هي غيره؟
قيل له : لأنه يكون تارةً مُستطيعًا وتارةً عاجزا، كما يكون تارةً عالما وتارةً غير عالم، وتارةً متحركًا وتارةً غير متحرك، فوجب أن يكونَ مُسْتطيعا بمعنى هو غيرُه، كما وجب أن يكون عالمًا بمعنى هو غيرُه، وكما وجب أن يكون متحركا بمعنى هو غيرُه؛ لأنه لو كانَ مُستطيعا بنفسه، أو بمعنى تستحيل مفارقَتُه لَهُ، لَمْ يُوجد إِلَّا وهُو مُستطيع، فلمَّا وُجِدَ مرَّةً مُستطيعا ومرَّةً غيرَ مُستطيع صح، وثبتَ أنَّ استطاعته غيرُه .

فإن قال قائل : فإذا أثبتُم له استطاعة هي غيره، فلمَ زعمتم أنه يستحيل تقدمها للفعل؟
قيل له : زعمنا ذلك من قبل أن الفعل لا يخلو : أن يكون حادثاً [مقارنتها الاستطاعة في حال حدوثها، أو بعدها]
Chapter on Discourse on Capacity

If someone says: Why do you say that man is capable by a capacity that is other than himself?
It is said to him: Because he is sometimes capable and sometimes incapable, just as he is sometimes knowing and sometimes not knowing, sometimes moving and sometimes not moving. So it must be that he is capable by a meaning that is other than himself, just as it must be that he is knowing by a meaning that is other than himself, and just as it must be that he is moving by a meaning that is other than himself. For if he were capable by himself, or by a meaning that is impossible to separate from him, he would never exist except as capable. Since he is found sometimes capable and sometimes not capable, it is correct and established that his capacity is other than himself.

If someone says: Since you have established for him a capacity that is other than himself, why do you claim that it is impossible for it to precede the act?
It is said to him: We claim that because the act must either be occurring [concurrent with the capacity at the time of its occurrence, or after it].
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage addresses two objections regarding human capacity (al-istita'a). First, the author proves that capacity is an accident (ʿaraḍ) distinct from the person, using an analogy with knowledge and motion. He argues that since a person alternates between being capable and incapable, capacity cannot be identical to the person (which is constant) or an inseparable attribute; it must be a separate, changeable attribute. The second objection questions why this capacity cannot precede the act. The answer (partially preserved) argues that the act coincides with the capacity or occurs after it, implying simultaneity or immediate conjunction—a standard Ashʿarī position that capacity does not exist before the act.

Key Terms
استطاعة
مستطيع
عاجز
قدرة
مقدور
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That human capacity is a permanent faculty that exists before the act and is identical to the person.
Correction: Al-Ashʿarī argues that capacity is a momentary accident that alternates with incapacity and only exists at the time of the act, not before.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, why must human capacity (istita'a) be something other than the person himself?
A) Because the person is always capable
B) Because the person alternates between being capable and incapable, unlike the person who is constant
C) Because the person cannot be the cause of his own acts
D) Because the person is composed of atoms

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's proof that capacity is an accident distinct from the person relies on the observed alternation between capability and incapability, analogous to knowledge and motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience moments of ability and inability every day. Do you truly acknowledge that your capacity is not your own but a created accident? If so, why do you still take pride in your achievements as if they were from yourself?
?
If your capacity to do good is a momentary gift, how should that change the way you approach the next sin you habitually commit? Will you rely on a pre-existing 'willpower' or beg the Creator for a fresh accident of ability?
?
Al-Ash'ari argues that if capacity were inherent, you would always be capable. But you know you fail. When you fail to obey Allah, do you blame your lack of 'ability'—or do you recognize that your inability itself is a created accident that Allah could replace? What does this teach you about your utter dependence on Him?

Limb vs. Power: The True Cause of Action
Al-Ash'ari distinguishes limb from power in causation of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's distinction between the limb and the power (qudra) as the true cause of action, and why the absence of a limb does not directly cause the absence of action.

Core Idea
Power (qudra) is the direct cause of action, not the limb.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari addresses an objection that seems to suggest that the existence of a limb is necessary for action. He argues that it is not the limb itself but the power residing in it that determines action. By analyzing counterfactual scenarios, he demonstrates that power is the decisive factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the structure of the argument. Notice that Al-Ash'ari first states the objection, then gives his response.
The objection is brief; the response is detailed.
2
Analyze the counterfactual arguments
Identify the two conditional statements al-Ash'ari uses: (1) if limb absent but power present, action occurs; (2) if limb present but power absent, no action. Understand how these prove his point.
These are logical proofs based on what would happen.
3
Draw the conclusion
Al-Ash'ari concludes that the absence of ability (istita'a) is the real reason for lack of action. Reflect on why this distinction matters for understanding human agency (kasb).
This connects to the broader doctrine of acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : أليس في عدم الجارحة عدم الفعل ؟ قيل لهم : في عدم الجارحة عدم القدرة ، وفي عدم القدرة عدم الاكتساب ؛ لأنها إذا عدمت عدمت القدرة ، فلعدم القدرة ما استحال الكسب ، إذا عدمت الجارحة ، لا لعدم الجارحة . ولو عدمت الجارحة ووجدت القدرة ، لكان الاكتساب واقعا ، ولو كان إنما استحال الاكتساب لعدم الجارحة ، لكانت إذا وجدت وجد الكسب . فلما كانت توجد ويقارنها العجز ، وتعدم القدرة فلا يكون كسب ، علم أن الاكتساب إنما لم يقع لعدم الاستطاعة لا لعدم الجارحة .
For if they say: 'Is it not that in the absence of the limb there is absence of the action?' It is said to them: 'In the absence of the limb there is absence of power, and in the absence of power there is absence of acquisition; because when it (the limb) is absent, power is absent, and due to the absence of power, acquisition becomes impossible when the limb is absent, not due to the absence of the limb. And if the limb were absent and power were present, acquisition would occur. And if acquisition had become impossible only due to the absence of the limb, then when it (the limb) is present, acquisition would be present. But since it (the limb) is present while incapacity accompanies it, and power is absent, so no acquisition occurs, it is known that acquisition only fails to occur due to the absence of ability (istita'a), not due to the absence of the limb.'
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that the absence of a limb (al-jāriḥa) directly causes the absence of action. Al-Ashʿarī responds by distinguishing between the limb and the power (qudra) to act. He argues that the limb's absence implies absence of power, and it is the absence of power that prevents action. He supports this with a conditional argument: if power were present without the limb, action would occur; and if the limb were present without power, action would not occur. Since we observe limbs present with incapacity (ʿajz) and without power, it is clear that the decisive factor is power (or ability, istiṭāʿa), not the limb itself.

Key Terms

قدرة
كسب
استطاعة
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Physical conditions like the presence of a limb directly cause action.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that action depends on power (qudra), not the limb itself. The limb is merely a vessel.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what is the real reason that the absence of a limb prevents action?
A) Because the limb is necessary for action.
B) Because the absence of the limb causes absence of power, which is necessary for action.
C) Because God directly prevents action when the limb is absent.
D) Because the limb is the cause of power.

Key Takeaway
The absence of action when a limb is missing is due to the absence of power, not the limb itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something, do you thank Allah solely for the outcome, or do you also acknowledge that He granted you the very power (qudra) to perform that action? How would your daily gratitude shift if you saw every movement as dependent on His ongoing gift of ability?
?
If you were to lose a limb tomorrow, would your belief that 'action requires power, not limb' give you hope that you could still earn rewards through other means? Or would you despair of being able to do anything? What does that reveal about your current reliance on your physical body versus reliance on Allah's boundless ability to grant capacity anywhere?
?
Consider a time you felt completely incapable of doing a good deed. Was your incapacity due to a lack of physical means, or a lack of inner resolve and reliance on Allah's power? After studying this lesson, are you willing to ask Allah for istita'a even when your limbs are intact but your heart feels paralyzed?

Life is Not the Direct Cause of Acquisition
Al-Ash'ari applies the same reasoning to life as a condition for action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari argues that life is a necessary condition for power, not a direct cause of human action (kasb).

Core Idea
Life enables power; power enables action.

This lesson continues al-Ash'ari's defense of kasb by addressing a new objection: if life is absent, acquisition is absent, so doesn't that make life the cause? Al-Ash'ari agrees that life is necessary but clarifies that it is only through power—which requires life—that action occurs. He provides a clear empirical example to separate life from power as the direct condition for action.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a question from the opponent, followed by al-Ash'ari's answer.
The opponent's reasoning seems logical—absence of life leads to absence of action. But al-Ash'ari refines the causal chain.
2
Identify the key distinction
Al-Ash'ari distinguishes between life and power. He admits that life's absence leads to power's absence, but insists that action is impossible due to power's absence, not life's.
This is a subtle but crucial point in the theory of kasb.
3
Examine the empirical evidence
Al-Ash'ari offers an observable case: a person can be alive but incapable (ʿajz) and thus unable to act. This shows that life alone is insufficient for action.
The example of incapacity (ʿajz) is key—it is the opposite of power.
4
Connect to the previous argument
Notice the final sentence: 'The answer regarding life is like the answer regarding the limb.' This links back to the earlier objection about limbs, showing consistency in al-Ash'ari's reasoning.
This reinforces that the direct condition for action is power, not any physical or biological factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : أفليس في عدم الحياة عدم الكسب ؟ قيل لهم : نعم ; لأن الحياة إذا عدمت عدمت القدرة , فلعدم القدرة ما استحال الكسب لا لعدم الحياة . ألا ترون أن الحياة تكون موجودة وثم عجز , فلا يكون الإنسان مكتسبا , فعلم أن الكسب لم يعدم لعدمها , ولا يوجد لوجودها . والجواب في الحياة كالجواب في الجارحة .

Do you not see that life may be present while there is incapacity, so the human is not an acquirer? Thus it is known that acquisition is not absent due to its (life's) absence, nor does it exist due to its presence. And the answer regarding life is like the answer regarding the limb.'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This is a second objection where the opponent argues that absence of life (ḥayāt) causes absence of action (kasb). Al-Ashʿarī agrees that life's absence leads to absence of power, but insists that it is the absence of power that causes failure of action, not life itself. He provides an empirical observation: life can exist while incapacity (ʿajz) is present, and in such a case no action occurs. Therefore, action is not tied to life but to power. The conclusion parallels the previous argument about the limb.

D) Life is the same as power.

Key Takeaway
Life is necessary for power, but power is the direct condition for human action (kasb).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Objection about Enabling and Power: Al-Ash'ari's Refutation
Al-Ash'ari refutes an objection about enabling and weaving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's argument that human power (qudra) is the direct cause of action, not God's enabling (ihsan).

Core Idea
Enabling does not necessitate action; power is the decisive factor.

Al-Ash'ari addresses an opponent's objection about the relationship between God's enabling and human action. The opponent claims that if lack of enabling causes lack of action, then enabling should cause action. Al-Ash'ari refutes this by pointing to cases where enabling is present but action does not occur due to incapacity. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the objection and response.
Notice the compact style of argumentation.
2
Identify the opponent's objection
The opponent uses a conditional: if absence of enabling causes absence of weaving, then presence of enabling should cause presence of weaving. Locate this in the text.
3
Analyze Al-Ash'ari's response
Al-Ash'ari denies the premise. He argues that weaving is absent due to absence of power, not enabling. He tests the opponent's logic: if enabling were the cause, then enabling would always produce weaving, but we see cases where enabling exists yet weaving does not (due to incapacity).
The key term 'ajz (incapacity) is contrasted with qudra (power).
4
Understand the hypothetical about God's custom
Al-Ash'ari says that if God were to create power without enabling, weaving would still occur. This shows that power alone is sufficient; enabling is not necessary.
This counters the idea that enabling is a prerequisite.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
This argument defends the Ash'ari view that God creates both power and action directly, and human acquisition (kasb) does not require a separate enabling act.
Consider how this relates to debates about predestination and free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إذا كان في عدم الإحسان للحياكة عدم الحياكة , فلم لا يكون في وجود الإحسان لها وجودها ؟ قيل : إن الحياكة تعدم لعدم قدرتها , لا لعدم إحسانها , ولو عدمت الحياكة لعدم الإحسان لها لوجدت بوجود الإحسان لها . فلما لم يكن ذلك كذلك , وكان الإحسان لها يجامعه العجز , علم أنها إنما تعدم لعدم القدرة عليها . ولو أجرى الله تعالى العادة أن يخلق القدرة عليها مع عدم الإحسان لها , لوقعت الحياكة لا محالة .
For if they say: 'Since in the absence of enabling (ihsan) for weaving there is absence of weaving, why is it that in the presence of enabling for it there is not its presence?' It is said: 'Weaving is absent due to the absence of its power, not due to the absence of its enabling. And if weaving were absent due to the absence of enabling for it, then it would exist with the existence of enabling for it. But since it is not so, and enabling for it is accompanied by incapacity (ʿajz), it is known that it is only absent due to the absence of power over it. And if God—exalted—were to make it His custom to create power over it alongside the absence of enabling for it, weaving would occur without fail.'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about the relationship between God's enabling (iḥsān) and human action, using weaving (ḥiyāka) as an example. The opponent argues that the absence of enabling causes the absence of weaving, so the presence of enabling should cause its presence. Al-Ashʿarī responds that weaving is absent because of lack of power (qudra), not lack of enabling. He argues hypothetically: if absence of enabling were the cause, then enabling would guarantee weaving. But we see cases where enabling exists yet incapacity (ʿajz) remains, so weaving does not occur. Therefore, the decisive factor is power, not enabling. He further states that if God miraculously created power without enabling, weaving would still occur, proving that power is sufficient.

B) Weaving would occur.
C) Weaving would only occur with enabling.

Key Takeaway
The presence of enabling does not guarantee action; power is the immediate cause, and God's custom can overrule typical patterns.

Objection about Freedom and Release (Takhliya wa Itlaq)
Al-Ash'ari agrees that freeing and release are necessary for action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Ash'ari affirms the necessity of freeing (takhliya) and release (itlaq) as conditions for action, while maintaining that power (qudra) is the direct cause.

Core Idea
Conditions are necessary, but not the direct cause.

In this brief exchange, al-Ash'ari responds to an objection about the role of freedom and release in action. The opponent argues that if their absence prevents action, their presence must cause it. Al-Ash'ari's simple agreement reveals an important nuance: he accepts the necessity of these conditions without conceding that they are the immediate cause. This lesson explores that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opponent's statement: 'Since in the absence of freeing and release there is absence of action, then in their presence there is presence of action.' Notice the logical structure: absence of X leads to absence of Y, so presence of X leads to presence of Y.
This is a conditional syllogism.
2
Examine al-Ash'ari's response
Al-Ash'ari says 'We say the same.' He agrees with the premise. This is not a denial but an affirmation that these conditions are necessary.
Why would he agree? Because he doesn't deny necessary conditions; he only insists that the direct cause is power.
3
Understand the theological nuance
Reflect on how this agreement fits with Ash'ari theology. It shows that al-Ash'ari does not reject secondary causality entirely; he only subordinates it to God's power as the primary agent.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the implication of al-Ash'ari's agreement.

For if they say: 'Since in the absence of freeing (takhliya) and release (itlaq) there is absence of action, then in their presence there is presence of action.' It is said to them: 'We say the same.'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This is a brief objection and answer. The opponent argues that the absence of freeing (takhliya) and release (itlaq) prevents action, so their presence should cause action. Al-Ashʿarī simply agrees: 'We say the same.' This indicates that he accepts the premise that these conditions are necessary for action, but he would still maintain that power (qudra) is the direct cause. The passage shows that the Ashʿarī position is not to deny all conditions but to insist that the immediate cause is power, while other conditions are indirect.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari agrees that takhliya and itlaq are necessary for action, but they are not the direct cause; God's power is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Objection and Answer: Why Doesn't Capability Guarantee Action?
Ash'ari answers an objection about capability and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between ability (إحسان) and power (قدرة) and why the presence of ability does not necessitate the action.

Core Idea
Ability can exist without power; only power directly produces action.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari addresses a common objection regarding divine capability and human action. The objector assumes that if lack of ability prevents an action, then having ability should guarantee it. Ash'ari's response clarifies a crucial distinction between two concepts: ability (إحسان) and power (قدرة). Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Focus on the objector's argument: if absence of ability prevents weaving, then presence of ability should produce weaving.
The objector uses the example of a weaver.
2
Identify the two terms
Notice how the answer distinguishes إحسان (ability) from قدرة (power). The answer claims that weaving is absent due to lack of power, not lack of ability.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
The answer shows that if the absence were due to ability, then presence of ability would always cause the action. But we observe that ability can coexist with incapacity, so that cannot be the case.
This disproves the objector's assumption.
4
Consider the hypothetical scenario
Finally, note the hypothetical: if Allah's custom were to create power even without ability, the action would necessarily occur. This reinforces that power is the direct cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is said: 'Weaving is absent due to the absence of power (قدرة) over it, not due to the absence of ability for it. Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer in the discussion of capability (استطاعة). The objector argues: if lack of ability (إحسان) leads to lack of action (weaving), then the presence of ability should lead to the presence of action. The answer distinguishes ability from power (قدرة). Weaving is absent because of lack of power, not lack of ability. If the absence were due to ability, then presence of ability would guarantee action. But we observe that ability can coexist with incapacity (عجز), proving that power is the real cause. The author adds a hypothetical: if Allah's custom were to create power even without ability, the action would occur necessarily. This shows that power is the direct cause, not ability.
