Two Objections on Conditions for Action
Ash'ari responds to two objections equating conditions with causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conditions like vacating and structural capacity are necessary but not sufficient for an action, and how Ash'ari's responses hinge on distinguishing conditions from efficient causes.

Core Idea
Conditions enable action, but they do not cause it.

In this passage, Ash'ari addresses two objections that attempt to turn necessary conditions into sufficient causes. The first concerns the removal of obstacles (vacating and releasing), the second concerns the inherent capacity of the body. Ash'ari's brief but consistent reply—'Similarly we say'—reveals a key principle of his theology: conditions are prerequisites, not producers. The act itself requires something else, namely power (qudra).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of the objections and answers.
Notice the repeated phrase 'قيل لهم : كذلك نقول' (It is said to them: Similarly we say).
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection links absence of action to absence of 'vacating and releasing'. The second links absence to lack of structural capacity. Both argue: if absence causes absence, then presence should cause presence.
The structure is parallel: condition A → action A; condition not A → action not A.
3
Understand Ash'ari's repeated answer
Ash'ari says 'Similarly we say' to both. He agrees that these are conditions, but he does not concede that they are sufficient. The act requires an additional efficient cause.
The term 'كسب' (acquisition) hints at the role of human power.
4
Grasp the final general rule
Ash'ari concludes that all such objections are answered by the same logic as the earlier case of the limb and life: the absence of the act is not due to the absence of these conditions, but due to the absence of something else (power).
This rule ties the passage to the broader Ash'ari theory of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فإذا كان في عدم التخلية والإطلاق عدم الفعل ففي وجودهما وجود الفعل.
قيل لهم : كذلك نقول.
فإن قالوا : فإذا كان في عدم احتمال البنية للفعل عدم الفعل، فلم لا يكون في وجود احتمال البنية للشيء وجوده ؟
قيل لهم : كذلك نقول؛ لأن البنية لا تحتمل إلا ما يقوم بها ، وكل ما تعارضونا به في هذه العلة فالجواب فيه كالجواب في الجارحة والحياة؛ لأنه ليس عدم الكسب لعدمه.
If they say: 'If the absence of vacating (تخلية) and releasing (إطلاق) entails the absence of the action, then in their presence is the presence of the action?' It is said to them: 'Similarly we say.' If they say: 'If the absence of the capacity (احتمال) of the structure (بنية) for the action entails the absence of the action, then why is it not that in the presence of the structure's capacity for the thing its presence [occurs]?' It is said to them: 'Similarly we say; because the structure only bears what subsists in it, and everything you oppose us with in this cause, the answer in it is like the answer regarding the limb (جارحة) and life (حياة), because the absence of acquisition (كسب) is not due to its absence.'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage contains two consecutive objections with brief answers. The first objection: if absence of 'vacating and releasing' (i.e., removal of obstacles) causes absence of action, then their presence should cause presence. The answer: 'Similarly we say'—meaning we agree that these are conditions, but they are not sufficient; something else is needed (power). The second objection: if lack of the structure's capacity to bear an action causes its absence, then presence of capacity should cause presence. The answer is again 'Similarly we say,' with an addition: the structure only bears what subsists in it (i.e., actions that inhere in it). Then Ash'ari gives a general rule: all such objections are answered by the same logic as the earlier answer regarding the limb (جارحة) and life (حياة)—the absence of the action (acquisition) is not due to the absence of these conditions, but due to the absence of power.

Key Terms
تخلية
إطلاق
احتمال
بنية
جارحة
حياة
كسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a condition is necessary for an action, then fulfilling that condition guarantees the action.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that conditions like vacating or capacity are necessary but not sufficient; the act requires an additional efficient cause (power).

Checkpoint
What is the general answer Ash'ari gives to objections about conditions like vacating or structure capacity?
A) Objections are invalid because the conditions are impossible
B) The same answer as given for the limb (جارحة) and life (حياة)
C) He admits the objection and changes his position
D) He says the conditions are irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Conditions like vacating and structural capacity are necessary prerequisites for action, but they are not sufficient; the act itself requires a separate power (qudra).

Qur'anic Evidence: Capability Arises with the Action
Ash'ari cites Quranic verses to prove capability is simultaneous with the action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses two Quranic verses to prove that capability to act is concurrent with the action, not prior to it.

Core Idea
Capability is not separate from the action—they occur together.

Imam al-Ash'ari now turns to the Qur'an to support the Ash'ari position on human capability. He presents two key verses that, when properly understood, show that capability (istita'ah) is not a precondition for obligation but arises simultaneously with the act. Read these proofs carefully—they also address the controversial issue of obligating what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that Ash'ari uses two distinct examples.
2
Analyze the first proof (Moses/Khidr)
Ash'ari argues from the absence of patience to the absence of capability. How does this show simultaneity?
The verse indicates that when the act (patience) is absent, capability is also absent.
3
Analyze the second proof (disbelievers not hearing)
Here the Quran states the disbelievers 'were not able to hear' yet they were commanded to hear. What does this imply about obligation and capability?
This shows that obligation can apply even when one is not capable (taklif ma la yutaq).
4
Synthesize the lesson
Combine both proofs to see the Ash'ari doctrine: capability exists exactly when the action exists, and it is possible to obligate a person who lacks capability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[أدلة النقل] ومما يدل على أن الاستطاعة مع الفعل : قول الخضر والموسى عليهما السلام : { إنك لن تستطيع معي صبرا } [الكهف: 67] ، فعلمنا أنه لما لم يصبر لم يكن للصبر مستطيعًا، وفي هذا بيان أن ما لم تكن استطاعة لم يكن الفعل ، وأنها إذا كانت كان لا محالة.
ومما يبين ذلك أن الله -تعالى- قال : { ما كانوا يستطيعون السمع } [هود: 20] ، وقال : { وكانوا لا يستطيعون سمعا } ، وقد أمروا أن يستمعوا الحق وكلفوه ، فدل ذلك على جواز تكليف ما لا يطاق، وأن من لم يقبل الحق ولم يسمعه على طريق القبول لم يكن مستطيعا.
[Textual proofs] Among what indicates that capability is with the action is the saying of al-Khidhr and Moses, peace be upon them: {Indeed, you will never be able to have patience with me} [Al-Kahf: 67]. So we know that because he did not have patience, he was not capable of patience. And in this is an explanation that whenever capability is not present, the action is not present, and that when it is present, it necessarily occurs.
And among what clarifies that is that Allah—exalted is He—said: {They were not able to hear} [Hud: 20], and He said: {And they were not able to hear}, and they were commanded to listen to the truth and were obligated with it. So that indicates the permissibility of obligating what cannot be borne (تكليف ما لا يطاق), and that whoever did not accept the truth and did not listen to it in the way of acceptance was not capable.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs from revelation (أدلة النقل) for the Ash'ari position that capability (استطاعة) is simultaneous with the action, not prior. The first proof: In the story of Moses and al-Khidr, Allah says Moses 'will never be able' to have patience, and indeed he did not have patience. This shows that the absence of capability coincided with the absence of the action, and conversely capability would have coincided with the action. The second proof: The Quran says the disbelievers 'were not able to hear' and yet they were commanded to listen. This proves that obligation can apply even when one is not capable (تكليف ما لا يطاق). Moreover, their inability to hear was not because the act was impossible in itself, but because they did not accept the truth. Thus capability is not a prerequisite for obligation; rather, capability arises along with the act itself.

Key Terms
استطاعة
تكليف ما لا يطاق
صبر
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That these verses imply determinism or that humans are coerced into actions.
Correction: Ash'ari uses them to argue that capability is concurrent with action, not that actions are forced. The verses show that inability arises from the person's own state, not from external compulsion.

Checkpoint
What does the verse about Moses and al-Khidr ("You will never be able to have patience") demonstrate according to Ash'ari?
A) That Moses was incapable of patience permanently
B) That capability is absent when the action is absent, and present when the action is present
C) That al-Khidr was a prophet
D) That patience is impossible

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an shows that capability exists only when the action exists, making it concurrent, and it is permissible to obligate what one cannot do (taklif ma la yutaq).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided an act of obedience because you felt you were not capable of it? How does this lesson's teaching—that capability arises with the action—challenge your excuse? What is one thing you can do today to act first, trusting that Allah will grant you the ability?

When you read that the disbelievers were commanded to hear but were not able to, do you see any similarity to your own heart when it resists the truth? In what area of your life are you 'not able to hear' what Allah is commanding you, and what would it cost you to actually listen?
?
This passage affirms that obligation is valid even when you feel incapable. How does that change the way you view your daily responsibilities toward Allah? Are there any obligations you have dismissed as too difficult? Write down one specific obligation you will undertake today despite feeling unable.

Objection on Preoccupation and Al-Ash'ari's Response
Al-Ash'ari presents an objection and refutes it by a parallel argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's method of responding to objections about ability by drawing a parallel that exposes the flaw in the opponent's reasoning.

Core Idea
Preoccupation is not a separate cause of inability; it is the same as rejecting truth.

This passage opens the chapter on istita'a with a direct objection. The opponents argue that people cannot accept faith because they are preoccupied. Al-Ash'ari responds not by denying preoccupation, but by showing that the objection merely restates their own position. This lesson explores that rebuttal and its implications for understanding divine power and human ability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opponent's claim: 'Are they not capable due to preoccupation?' Understand the assumption that preoccupation negates ability.
2
Identify the response
Al-Ash'ari responds with a question: What is the difference between you and someone who says they are unable because of preoccupation with abandoning truth?
Note the parallel structure.
3
Analyze the logic
The response shows that the objection is circular: the opponent's claim is just a rewording of their own position. If one is valid, so is the other.
This exposes the weakness in the objection.
4
Reflect on the method
Al-Ash'ari uses parity (qiyas al-tard) to refute without adding new premises. This is a common kalam technique.
Think about how this method protects the doctrine of divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الاستطاعة فإن قالوا : لا يستطيعون للاشتغال؟ قيل لهم : ما الفرق بينكم وبين من قال : إنهم لا يستطيعون قبول الحق للاشتغال بتركه؟
Chapter on Speech about Ability. For they said: Are they not capable due to preoccupation? It was said to them: What is the difference between you and the one who says: They are not capable of accepting the truth due to preoccupation with abandoning it?
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a new chapter on istita'a and presents another objection-and-answer. The opponents claim that people are not able to accept faith because they are preoccupied with something else, suggesting that ability is separate and contingent on mental state. Al-Ash'ari responds by drawing a parallel: they could just as well say that people are not able because they are preoccupied with abandoning the truth. This parity exposes that the objection is merely a rephrasing of their own assertion, providing no new grounds. The response demonstrates that the inability is not due to a lack of capacity but due to their own choice of preoccupation.

Key Terms
الاستطاعة (al-istita'a)
الاشتغال (al-ishtighal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People cannot accept truth because they are too busy, so they have a valid excuse.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that this 'preoccupation' is actually their own choice of rejecting truth, so it is not an external constraint on ability.

Checkpoint

B) He argues that preoccupation is not a valid reason.
C) He draws a parallel with those who say they are unable due to preoccupation with abandoning truth.
D) He cites a Quranic verse.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari refutes the objection by showing that if preoccupation with one thing excuses inability, then the same logic applies to the opponent's own position.

Refuting the Capacity Objection
Ash'ari rebuts the claim that occupation prevents capacity for faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari uses a rhetorical question to refute the claim that occupation with disbelief prevents capacity for faith.

Core Idea
Occupation with disbelief does not negate capacity for faith.

Imam al-Ash'ari engages a key objection in kalām: if someone is occupied with disbelief, can they be said to have the capacity (istiṭāʿa) for faith? The opponent says no. Ash'ari's rebuttal is sharp and economical—a single rhetorical question that reveals the logical inconsistency. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage from the matn once, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Notice the concise style typical of Ash'ari's kalām.
2
Identify the two positions
Pinpoint the opponent's claim ('they cannot believe due to occupation') and Ash'ari's responsive question. Understand what each side asserts.
3
Analyze the logical move
Why does Ash'ari's rhetorical question expose an inconsistency? Consider: if the opponent's logic were sound, it would also apply to a believer occupied with truth. This self-defeat is the core of the rebuttal.
Ask yourself: does occupation with any act negate capacity for its opposite?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الاستطاعة فإن قالوا : لا يستطيعون للاشتغال؟ قيل لهم : ما الفرقُ بينكم وبينَ مَنْ قالَ : إِنَّهم لا يستطيعون قبول الحقِّ للاشتغال بتركه ؟

Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a brief objection and rebuttal concerning the capacity (istita'a) of the disbeliever to have faith. The opponent claims that the disbeliever cannot believe because he is occupied with disbelief. Imam Ash'ari responds with a rhetorical question: what distinguishes this claim from the claim that one cannot accept truth because he is occupied with rejecting it? The implication is that the same reasoning would apply to the believer who is occupied with accepting truth, undermining the objection's consistency. The argument thus exposes a logical flaw in the opponent's reasoning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being occupied with something negates the capacity to do something else.
Correction: Occupation with one act does not logically negate the capacity for another; otherwise, the believer occupied with faith could be said to lack capacity for its opposite.

D) He cites a Qur'anic verse.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Ash'ari refutes the capacity objection by exposing its logical inconsistency: the same reasoning would apply to the believer, making the objection self-defeating.

Incapacity vs. Neglect: The Disbeliever's Failure to Believe
Ash'ari clarifies the distinction between incapacity and neglect in a dialectical exchange.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ash'ari's distinction between genuine incapacity (ʿajz) and mere neglect (tark), and why the disbeliever's failure to believe is not due to incapacity.

Core Idea
Neglect does not equal incapacity—the disbeliever could believe if he chose.

This passage from Ash'ari's Al-Luma tackles a key objection in the debate on human capacity. The opponent argues that since the disbeliever does not believe, he must have been commanded something impossible. Ash'ari clarifies that there is a crucial difference between not being able to do something due to incapacity and not doing it because one is occupied with its opposite. Let's follow the dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the question-and-answer format.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'cannot'
The passage distinguishes between incapacity (ʿajz) and neglect (tark). Pinpoint where Ash'ari makes this distinction.
The answer is at 'هذا كلام يفهم على أمرين'.
3
Understand the definition of incapacity
Focus on the final line: Ash'ari defines true incapacity. Ask why neglect does not qualify as incapacity.
Incapacity means inability to do the act or its opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
29- مسألة فإن قال قائل : أليسَ قَدْ كَلَّفَ اللَّهُ -تعالى- الكافِرَ الإيمانَ؟ قلنا له : نعم . فإن قال : فيستطيع الإيمان؟ قيل له : لَوِ استطاعه لآمَنَ . فإن قال : فَكَلَّفَه ما لا يستطيعُ ؟ قيل له : هذا كلام يفهم على أمرين : إِنْ أَرَدْتَ بقولِكَ : إنَّه لا يستطيعُ الإيمان لعجزِهِ عَنْهُ فَلَا، وإِنْ أَرَدْتَ أنه لا يَستطِيعُه لتَرْكه واشتغاله بِضِدّه فَنَعَمْ . فإن قالُوا : مَا أَنكَرْتُم أن يكونَ الله - تعالى - كَلَّفَ الكافِرَ مَا يَعْجَرُ عنه لتَرْكِهِ له؟ قيل له : العَجْزُ عنِ الشَّيء أنَّه يخرج عنه وعن ضِدّه، فلذلكَ استحال أن يعجِزَ العاجز عن الشيء لتركه لَهُ .
29- Question: If a questioner says: 'Did Allah Exalted command the disbeliever with faith?' We say to him: 'Yes.' Then he says: 'So does he have capacity for faith?' It is said to him: 'If he had capacity for it, he would have believed.' Then he says: 'So He commanded him what he cannot do?' It is said to him: 'This statement is understood in two ways: If you mean by your saying that he cannot have faith due to his incapacity for it, then no; and if you mean that he cannot do it due to his abandonment and occupation with its opposite, then yes.' Then if they say: 'What prevents you from saying that Allah Exalted commanded the disbeliever what he is incapable of due to his abandonment of it?' It is said to him: 'Incapacity regarding a thing means that one departs from it and from its opposite; therefore it is impossible that one who is incapable of a thing due to his abandonment of it [be truly incapable].'
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (objection and answer) about whether the disbeliever is commanded to do something beyond his capacity. The opponent first asks: Did Allah command the disbeliever to have faith? Yes. Then does he have the capacity (istita'a) for it? Ash'ari responds: if he had capacity he would have believed. The opponent then says: so He commanded him what he cannot do? Ash'ari clarifies that this statement is ambiguous. He explains two meanings: (1) If by 'cannot' you mean genuine incapacity ('ajz) preventing the act entirely—that is not the case, because the disbeliever could believe if he chose; (2) If you mean he does not do it because he is occupied with its opposite (disbelief)—that is true. Then the opponent objects: why can't Allah have commanded something the disbeliever is incapable of (due to his neglect)? Ash'ari defines incapacity as being unable to do the thing or its opposite. Since a neglectful person could still do the opposite (if he abandoned his neglect), his not doing it stems from choice, not incapacity. Therefore, it is impossible for someone to be truly incapable due to neglect, because neglect implies potential to act otherwise.

Key Terms
استطاعة
عجز
ترك
اشتغال
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disbeliever's failure to believe shows he was incapable of believing.
Correction: Ash'ari argues that failure to believe does not imply incapacity; it may stem from neglect and occupation with its opposite.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari, what is the correct understanding of 'the disbeliever cannot believe'?
A) He cannot believe because he lacks the capacity created by Allah.
B) He cannot believe due to incapacity, not mere neglect.
C) He cannot believe because he is occupied with disbelief, not because of incapacity.
D) He can believe but chooses not to, so he truly cannot.

Key Takeaway
Neglect does not equal genuine incapacity; the disbeliever's failure to believe is due to his choice, not lack of ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to act on a truth you know, do you excuse yourself by thinking 'I just can't' instead of admitting 'I didn't want to'? What would change if you stopped hiding behind self-deception?
?
In your own life, where have you been like the disbeliever—neglecting something you know is right, then blaming circumstances as if you were incapable? Will you today admit it was your choice, and turn back?
?
If you truly believe that neglect is not incapacity, how does that hold you accountable for the good deeds you keep postponing? What one thing will you stop pretending you cannot do and actually do today?

God's Command to the Disbeliever and Its Possibility
Imam Ashari addresses whether God commands what cannot be done.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on whether God commands what cannot be done, and the crucial distinction between incapacity and neglect.

Core Idea
God's command to the disbeliever is just because he has the ability to believe but chooses not to.

This passage presents a classic theological objection: if God commands the disbeliever to believe, does that mean He commands what is impossible? Imam Ashari responds by clarifying two meanings of 'cannot'. Understanding this distinction is essential for preserving both divine justice and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation. Notice the back-and-forth dialogue style.
The passage is structured as an objection and response.
2
Identify the objection
The questioner argues: if God commands the disbeliever to believe, then either he can or cannot believe. If he can, why doesn't he? If he cannot, God commands the impossible. Pinpoint this logic.
3
Understand the two meanings of 'cannot'
Ashari distinguishes: the disbeliever is not incapable due to lack of power, but he does not believe because he chooses disbelief. Grasp this distinction as the core of the answer.
Incapacity (عجز) vs. neglect (ترك).
4
Reflect on the theological consequences
Consider why this distinction matters: it defends God's justice while affirming human free will and accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: أليس قد كلف الله تعالى الكافر الإيمان؟ قلنا له: نعم. فإن قال: فيستطيع الإيمان؟ قيل له: لو استطاعه لآمن. فإن قال: فكلفه ما لا يستطيع؟ قيل له: هذا كلام يفهم على أمرين: إن أردت بقولك: إنه لا يستطيع الإيمان لعجزه عنه فلا، وإن أردت أنه لا يستطيعه لتركه واشتغاله بضده فنعم.
If a questioner says: 'Has not God Most High commanded the disbeliever to believe?' We say to him: 'Yes.' If he says: 'Then is he able to believe?' It is said to him: 'If he were able to believe, he would have believed.' If he says: 'Then He commanded him what he cannot do?' It is said to him: 'This statement is understood in two ways: If you mean by your statement that he cannot believe due to his incapacity for it, then no; and if you mean that he cannot do it due to his leaving it and occupying himself with its opposite, then yes.'
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents a theological objection and its answer. The objector argues that if God commands the disbeliever to believe, then either the disbeliever can believe or cannot. If he can, why doesn't he? If he cannot, then God commands what is impossible, which is unjust. Imam Ashari responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'cannot'. He explains that the disbeliever's lack of belief is not due to an inherent incapacity, but due to his free choice to occupy himself with disbelief. Thus, he is able to believe in the sense of having the capacity, but he does not do so because he chooses otherwise. This preserves God's justice while affirming human responsibility.

Key Terms
تكليف
استطاعة
عجز
إيمان
كافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'cannot' here implies physical impossibility, meaning the disbeliever inherently lacks the ability to believe.
Correction: Ashari clarifies that the disbeliever's inability is due to his own choice to neglect belief and occupy himself with its opposite, not due to any lack of capacity.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Ashari, what does it mean when the disbeliever 'cannot' believe?
A) He lacks the physical ability to believe.
B) He has the ability but chooses not to believe.
C) God prevents him from believing.
D) He is inherently incapable of belief.

Key Takeaway
The disbeliever's failure to believe is due to his own choice, not lack of capacity, preserving God's justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay or abandon an act of obedience, do you subtly justify yourself by thinking 'I just can't do it'? How does this lesson expose the difference between genuine incapacity and mere choice? What will you do the next time you catch yourself using that excuse?
?
If you truly believe that every command of God is within your power—but your own turning away is what prevents you—then what does that say about the urgency of your next moment of hesitation? Will you treat that hesitation as a death sentence or a call to action?
?
This lesson teaches that the disbeliever is accountable because he could have believed but chose otherwise. What 'lesser disbelief' or negligence do you harbor, knowing full well you have the capacity to change? What specific step will you take today to stop occupying yourself with its opposite?

Refuting the Analogy: The Counter-Example of Assistance
Imam Ashari presents a counter-analogy using assistance (al-‘awn) to refute the analogy between power and incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ashari employs a counter-analogy to break the false symmetry between capability and incapacity.

Core Idea
If power were like incapacity, then assistance would be like power — an absurd conclusion.

The objector tries to draw a parallel between capability and incapacity: just as one incapable of a thing is also incapable of its opposite, so one capable of a thing should be capable of its opposite. Imam Ashari begins his refutation by pointing out that such an analogy would also apply to the term 'assistance' (al-‘awn), leading to an obvious falsehood. This lesson explores the first move in Ashari's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objector's claim
Read the first line of the passage where the objector presents the analogy between power and incapacity.
Notice the structure: 'just as ... so ...'
2
Identify Ashari's counter-example
Ashari introduces the concept of 'assistance' (al-‘awn) to show that the analogy fails. Find the line where he says 'if strength over a thing were strength over its opposite...'
He uses the same pattern but with 'assistance' to create a parallel that is obviously false.
3
Understand why the counter-example works
Explain that assistance cannot be over both a thing and its opposite, so the analogy is invalid. This breaks the initial claim.
The key is that the analogy is not universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: ما أنكرت أن يكون القادر على الشيء قادرًا على ضده، كما كان العاجز عن الشيء عاجزًا عن ضده؟ قيل له: لو كانت القوة على الشيء قوة على ضده، قياسًا على العجز، للزم أن يكون العون على الشيء عونًا على ضده؛ قياسًا على أن العجز عن الشيء عجز عن ضده. وأيضًا: فلو كانت القدرة على الشيء قدرة على ضده، قياسًا على العجز؛ لأن العجز عن الشيء عجز عن ضده، لوجب في القدرة ما وجب في العجز، من أنه يتأتى بها الشيء وضده، كما يتعذر بالعجز الشيء وضده. ولو كان العجز إذا وجد عدم الشيء وضده، المعجوز عنهما، مع وجوده، فلم يكن الإنسان مكتسبًا لهما، لكان يلزم في القدرة مثله إذا وجدت، وهي القدرة على الشيء وضده أن يوجد الشيء وضده معها.
If he says: 'Why do you deny that the one who is capable of a thing is capable of its opposite, just as the one who is incapable of a thing is incapable of its opposite?' It is said to him: 'If strength over a thing were strength over its opposite, by analogy with incapacity, then it would necessarily follow that assistance (al-‘awn) over a thing is assistance over its opposite, by analogy with the fact that incapacity over a thing is incapacity over its opposite. Also: if power over a thing were power over its opposite, by analogy with incapacity—because incapacity over a thing is incapacity over its opposite—then what is necessary in incapacity would be necessary in power: that the thing and its opposite can be accomplished by it, just as the thing and its opposite are impossible by incapacity. And if incapacity, when it occurs, necessitates the absence of the thing and its opposite—the two things of which one is incapable—while it (incapacity) exists, so that a person does not acquire them, then it would necessarily follow in power, when it exists—and it is power over the thing and its opposite—that the thing and its opposite would exist with it.'
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage continues the debate. The objector argues that just as one who is incapable of a thing is also incapable of its opposite, similarly, one who is capable of a thing should be capable of its opposite. Imam Ashari refutes this by first employing a counter-analogy: if ability were analogous to inability, then 'assistance' (al-‘awn) would also follow the same pattern, but it does not. Then he presents a reductio: if power over a thing implied power over its opposite, then the same would hold for incapacity: incapacity over a thing would entail incapacity over its opposite, which is false. Actually, incapacity over a thing does not entail incapacity over its opposite. The argument is complex but essentially breaks the false symmetry. Ashari also introduces the concept of 'acquisition' (kasb) to show that incapacity prevents both actions, so by analogy, power would necessitate both actions, which is absurd. Thus, the initial analogy fails.

قدرة
عجز
ضد
عون
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that capability for an action automatically extends to its opposite.
Correction: Ashari shows that this is not logically necessary by using a counter-example with assistance, which cannot apply to opposites.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Ashari use as a counter-example to refute the analogy between power and incapacity?
A) The concept of incapacity itself
B) The concept of assistance (al-‘awn)
C) The concept of acquisition (kasb)
D) A Quranic verse

Key Takeaway
Not all parallels hold: the objector's analogy between power and incapacity collapses when tested with the concept of assistance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Allah's decree that you have never seriously questioned. Could it be based on an analogy that, when tested, leads to an absurd conclusion? Will you examine it with the same logical rigor Ashari used today?

Are there any of your own arguments or justifications for your actions that, if carried to their logical end, would lead to contradiction or absurdity? Will you honestly trace them today?

Refuting the Analogy: The Reductio Ad Absurdum
Imam Ashari presents a reductio ad absurdum to show that the analogy between power and incapacity leads to impossible consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ashari uses a reductio ad absurdum to further dismantle the claim that power over a thing implies power over its opposite.

Core Idea
If power were like incapacity, it would necessitate both a thing and its opposite — an impossibility.

After breaking the analogy with the counter-example of assistance, Imam Ashari deepens his refutation by showing that the objector's reasoning leads to an absurd consequence. If power truly mirrored incapacity, then power over a thing would require power over its opposite, and the result would be that a person could simultaneously produce both — which is impossible. This lesson unpacks that reductio.

Guided Path
1
Read the reductio argument
Read the second part of the passage starting with 'Also: if power over a thing...'
Notice Ashari uses the phrase 'لوجب في القدرة ما وجب في العجز'.
2
Understand the logical move
Ashari argues that if the analogy held, then the same consequences would follow for power as for incapacity: where incapacity makes both impossible, power would make both possible. That is absurd.
3
Connect to the concept of acquisition
Ashari brings in the concept of acquisition (kasb) to show that incapacity prevents acquisition of both, so power would necessitate acquisition of both — but humans do not acquire opposites simultaneously.
This shows the analogy fails when applied to human action.

Source Text

If he says: 'Why do you deny that the one who is capable of a thing is capable of its opposite, just as the one who is incapable of a thing is incapable of its opposite?' Refuting the Analogy of Power with Incapacity
Al-Ash'ari refutes the analogy between power and incapacity regarding opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari rejects the analogy between power (qudra) and incapacity (ʿajz) concerning opposites, and how he uses reductio ad absurdum to invalidate it.

Core Idea
Power and incapacity are not analogous because they have opposite effects on opposites.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari responds to an opponent who argues that just as incapacity over a thing implies incapacity over its opposite, so power over a thing should imply power over its opposite. Al-Ash'ari dismantles this analogy by showing that if it were valid, it would lead to absurd consequences that violate the logical impossibility of two opposites coexisting. This lesson walks through his refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's objection
Begin by carefully reading the opponent's claim: that power over a thing should imply power over its opposite, based on an analogy with incapacity. Understand the parallel they are drawing.
Notice that the opponent assumes that power and incapacity share a similar logical structure.
2
Trace the first counter-analogy
Examine al-Ash'ari's first response: if the analogy held, it would also apply to assistance ('awn), leading to an absurd consequence. This shows the analogy cannot be applied universally.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: extending the opponent's logic to a ridiculous case.
3
Understand the main argument about opposites
Now focus on the detailed argument: if power were like incapacity, then power would necessitate the existence of both opposites simultaneously, which is impossible. In contrast, incapacity causes the absence of both. Therefore, the analogy fails.
The key difference: incapacity can cause non-existence of both opposites, but power can only bring about one at a time.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Al-Ash'ari concludes that the analogy is invalid because there is no common cause (ʿilla) uniting power and incapacity; they are different in genus. The opponent's objection is nullified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الاستطاعة
فإن قال : ما أنكرتَ أن يكونَ القادِرُ على الشيء قادرًا على ضده، كما كان العاجِزُ عن الشَّيءِ عاجزا عن ضِدّه .
قِيلَ لَهُ : لَوْ كَانَتِ القوةُ على الشَّيء قوة على ضده، قياسًا على العَجْزِ، لَلَزِمَ أن يكون العون على الشيء عونًا على ضدّه ؛ قياسًا على أن العجز عن الشيء عجز عن ضدّه .
وأيضًا : فَلَو كانتِ القُدرة على الشيء قدرة على ضِدّه، قياسًا على العجز؛ لأنَّ العجز عن الشيء عَجْزٌ عَنْ ضِدّه، لَوَجَبَ فِي القُدرةِ ما وَجَبَ في العجزِ، من أنَّه يَتَأَنَّى بها الشيءُ وضده، كما يتعذَّرُ بالعَجْزِ الشَّيءُ وضده. ولو كان العَجْزُ إِذا وُجِدَ عُدِمَ الشَّيءُ وضِدُّه، المعجوز عنهما، مع وُجُودِه، فلم يكن الإنسانُ مكتسباً لهما، لكانَ يَلْزمُ في القدرةِ مثلُهُ إِذا وُجِدَتْ، وهي القدرة على الشَّيء وضدّه أَنْ يُوجَدَ الشَّيءُ وضِدُّه معها وذلك مُحال؛ لأنَّه إِنَّما يَجبُ وُجودُ أَحَدِ الصّدينِ مع وُجودِها، بخلافِ ما نحكُمُ به في العجزِ؛ لأنَّ العجز يُحكمُ فيه بعدم المعجوز عنه وضده مع وُجودِه .
فإِنْ لم يَجُز هذا فَقَدْ بَطَلتِ العلَّةُ، وانتقضت المعارضَةُ، ولم يَجِبْ أن تُقاس القدرة على العجز، إذ لم تكن علة تجمع بينهما، ولم تكن القدرة من جنس العجز .
Chapter on discourse about ability (al-istiṭāʿa). If he says: What prevents you from saying that the one who has power (al-qādir) over a thing has power over its opposite (ḍidd), just as the one who is incapable (al-ʿājiz) of a thing is incapable of its opposite? It is said to him: If power (al-quwwa) over a thing were power over its opposite, by analogy (qiyās) with incapacity (al-ʿajz), then it would necessarily follow that assistance (al-ʿawn) in a thing is assistance in its opposite, by analogy with the fact that incapacity concerning a thing is incapacity concerning its opposite. And also: If power (al-qudra) over a thing were power over its opposite, by analogy with incapacity, because incapacity concerning a thing is incapacity concerning its opposite, then what holds for incapacity would have to hold for power, namely that the thing and its opposite occur through it, just as the thing and its opposite are impossible (yataʿadhdharu) through incapacity. And if incapacity, when it exists, causes the absence of the thing and its opposite, the two objects of incapacity, despite its existence, so that man does not acquire (muktasiban) either of them, then the like would have to hold for power, when it exists, and it is the power over the thing and its opposite, that the thing and its opposite should exist with it. And that is impossible (muḥāl), because only one of the two opposites (al-ḍiddayn) must exist with it, unlike what we judge in the case of incapacity, because in incapacity we judge the non-existence of the object of incapacity and its opposite despite its existence. Then if this is not permissible, then the cause (al-ʿilla) is invalidated and the objection (al-muʿāraḍa) is nullified, and it is not necessary to analogize power to incapacity, since there is no cause that unites them, and power is not of the same genus (jins) as incapacity.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent raises an objection based on an analogy: just as incapacity (ʿajz) over a thing implies incapacity over its opposite, so power (qudra) over a thing should imply power over its opposite. Al-Ash'ari responds by refuting the analogy. First, he points out that if this analogy held, it would also apply to assistance (ʿawn), which is absurd. Then he provides a more detailed argument: if power were like incapacity, then just as incapacity makes both a thing and its opposite impossible, power would make both possible. However, this is impossible because power can only bring about one of the two opposites at a time (since two opposites cannot coexist), whereas incapacity can be the cause of both not existing. Therefore, the analogy fails because there is no common cause (ʿilla) that unites power and incapacity; they are different in genus (jins). The opponent's objection is thus invalidated, and the argument that power over a thing implies power over its opposite is rejected.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
العجز (al-ʿajz)
الضد (al-ḍidd)
القياس (al-qiyās)
العلة (al-ʿilla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that power over a thing automatically implies power over its opposite by analogy with incapacity.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows the analogy is faulty because power and incapacity have opposite effects: incapacity causes absence of both opposites, while power can only bring about one. Thus the analogy does not hold.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ash'ari reject the analogy between power (qudra) and incapacity (ʿajz) regarding opposites?
A) Because incapacity causes the absence of only one opposite, not both.
B) Because if the analogy held, power would necessitate the existence of both opposites simultaneously, which is impossible.
C) Because power and incapacity share a common cause that unites them.
D) Because power is a subset of incapacity.

Key Takeaway
The analogy between power and incapacity over opposites is invalid because it would require the impossible coexistence of two opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your power over a good action does not automatically give you power over its opposite (e.g., to avoid sin), how does that make you reliant on Allah's assistance to do good and avoid evil? Will you today specifically ask Allah to grant you power only for what pleases Him, recognizing your inability to prevent the opposite on your own?
?
If you have ever assumed that you have full control over both obeying and disobeying Allah, how might that assumption lead to arrogance or a false sense of security? In light of this lesson, what specific steps will you take to humble yourself and acknowledge that your power is limited and given by Allah?
?
Al-Ash'ari refutes the idea that power over one opposite implies power over the other by showing the impossibility of both opposites coexisting. In your own life, when you are tempted to think you can both fulfill a duty and commit its opposite (e.g., pray on time and delay intentionally), what does this argument teach you about the nature of your ability? Will you repent from any dual intentions and commit your power solely to what Allah commands?

Ash'ari's Argument Against Obligating the Impossible
Ash'ari argues against obligating the impossible via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ash'ari's reductio ad absurdum argument that God does not command what is beyond a person's capacity, based on the absurd consequence of commanding with total ignorance.

Core Idea
God's commands are just and do not require the impossible.

Imam Ash'ari tackles a crucial theological question: does God ever command something a person is incapable of doing? Using a logical argument, he shows that such a view leads to absurdities. This passage presents his argument with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation, to familiarize yourself with the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the examples of limb and incapacity.
2
Identify the reductio structure
Notice how Ash'ari starts with a premise (commanding with partial inability implies commanding with total inability), draws an analogous case (partial ignorance implies total ignorance), and concludes the premise is false due to absurdity.
This is a classic logical demonstration.
3
Apply to other cases
Consider how Ash'ari generalizes the argument to all instances of obligating the impossible, such as zakat without wealth.
The same reasoning applies uniformly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الاستطاعة. ومع عدم الجارحة لا يوجد أخذ ولا ترك. وكذلك العجز لا يوجد معه أخذ ولا ترك؛ لأن العجز عجز عن الشيء وعن ضده. وأيضًا فلو وجب إذا أمر الله تعالى الإنسان بالشيء مع عدم قدرته عليه أن يأمر به مع عدم القدرة كلها، لوجب: إذا أمر الله تعالى الإنسان، مع عدم بعض العلوم، وهو العلم بالله تعالى وبأنه أمره أن يأمره بالفعل مع عدم العلوم كلها. فإن لم يجب هذا، لم يجب إذا أمر الإنسان، مع عدم القدرة على ما أمره به، أن يأمر مع عدم الجارحة التي إذا عدمت عدمت القدرة كلها، ومع وجود العجز الذي لم تعدم القدرة بوجوده. قال الشيخ أبو الحسن رحمه الله: وكل مسألة في تكليف ما لا يطاق من الأمر بالزكاة مع عدم المال، وغير ذلك من المسائل، فالجواب عنه كما أجبت به عن سؤالهم عن الأمر مع عدم الجارحة، والتكليف مع وجود العجز.
Chapter on Speech about Capacity. With the absence of a limb, there is no taking or leaving. Likewise, with incapacity, there is no taking or leaving with it; because incapacity is inability for the thing and for its opposite. Also, if it were necessary when God—Exalted—commands a person to something while he lacks ability for it that He command him while lacking all ability, then it would be necessary: when God—Exalted—commands a person while lacking some knowledge—which is knowledge of God—Exalted—and that He commanded him—that He command him to the act while lacking all knowledge. If this is not necessary, then it is not necessary, when He commands a person while lacking ability for what He commanded him, that He command while lacking the limb by whose absence all ability is absent, and with the existence of incapacity by whose existence ability is not absent. Al-Shaykh Abu al-Hasan—may God have mercy on him—said: And every issue in obligating what cannot be borne, such as the command for zakat while lacking wealth and other such issues, the answer for it is as I answered for their question about the command while lacking the limb, and obligation with the existence of incapacity.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on capacity (istita'a) by stating two foundational observations: absence of a limb prevents action (taking or leaving), and incapacity (ajz) likewise prevents action because it is inability for both the thing and its opposite. Ash'ari then employs a reductio ad absurdum argument: if it were obligatory for God to command a person to an act when the person lacks ability for that specific act (and thus command with total inability), then it would also follow that when commanding a person deficient in some knowledge (like knowledge of God and His command), God would have to command with total ignorance. Since the latter is absurd, the former premise is false. He extends this logic: if commanding with partial incapacity did not oblige commanding with total incapacity, then similarly, commanding with absence of a limb (which entails total incapacity) does not oblige commanding with total incapacity even when incapacity exists but does not remove all ability. Finally, he generalizes: all cases of obligating the impossible (e.g., zakat without wealth) are answered by the same reasoning applied to the limb and incapacity examples.

Key Terms
استطاعة
عجز
قدرة
تكليف ما لا يطاق
أخذ
ترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this argument denies God's ability to command anything.
Correction: Rather, it affirms that God does not command what a person cannot possibly do in a just manner. God's commands are always within capacity.

Checkpoint
What does Ash'ari argue about commanding a person lacking ability for a specific act?
A) It is permissible because God can do anything.
B) It would lead to the absurdity of commanding without all knowledge.
C) It is impossible because the person will be punished unfairly.
D) It is recommended as a test of faith.

Key Takeaway
God's commands are always within a person's capacity, and any apparent command to do the impossible is misinterpreted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from God that seems beyond your capacity, do you trust that He knows your limits, or do you feel burdened and resentful? How does Ash'ari's reasoning challenge your assumptions about God's expectations?
?
Have you ever excused yourself from an act of worship because you felt it was too difficult, using the excuse that God wouldn't require the impossible? How does this passage confront that excuse and call you to re-evaluate your assessment of your own capacity?
?
Reflect on a time when you believed God's command was unreasonable given your circumstances. According to this lesson, what is the true nature of God's commands? How might that change your response to such feelings in the future?

Refutation of Two Inabilities
Ash'ari refutes the claim that absence of a thing and its opposite is due to two inabilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari argues that inability is a single, unified attribute and cannot be multiple distinct inabilities.

Core Idea
Distinct inabilities lead to infinite regress and contradictory life-death states.

In this short but powerful passage, Imam Ash'ari tackles a subtle objection: could the absence of a thing and its opposite be due to two separate inabilities? He shows that such a view unravels into logical absurdities, thereby defending the unity of incapacity in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation to get the flow of the argument. Note the objector's claim and Ash'ari's immediate reply.
Notice the phrase 'fain qāla qā'il' marking the objection.
2
Analyze the first argument: infinite regress
Ash'ari argues that if inabilities were distinct, there would be infinite inabilities in a person, which is impossible. Try to understand why distinct inabilities would lead to infinity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze the second argument: death and simultaneous opposites
Using death as the greatest inability, Ash'ari shows that distinct inabilities would imply multiple deaths in one part, allowing one death to be removed while another remains, leading to the impossible state of being simultaneously alive and dead.
This reinforces the contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: ما أنكرتم أن يعدم الشيء وضده لوجود عجزين؟ قيل له: لأنه نهاية لما يعجز عنه الإنسان العاجز الذي لا قدرة فيه. فلو كان العجز عن كل شيء غير العجز عن غيره؛ لكان في الإنسان من الأعجاز ما لا يتناهى، وهذا محال. وأيضًا: فإن الموت هو أكبر الأعجاز؛ لأنه تتعذر معه الأفعال كلها، فلو كان العجز عن كل شيء غير العجز عن غيره، لكان بعض الميتين إنما تعدم منه الأفعال لوجود أعجاز، وهذا يوجب أن في الجزء الواحد عجزين وموتين، ولو جاز هذا لجاز أن يرتفع أحدهما إلى حياة، فيكون الجزء الواحد حيًا ميتًا في حال معًا، وهذا محال. فلما استحال هذا علم أنه محال قول من قال: إن العجز عن كل شيء غير العجز عن غيره، وبالله التوفيق.
If someone says: What prevents you that a thing and its opposite are absent due to the existence of two inabilities? It is said to him: Because it is the utmost of what the incapable person who has no ability can be incapable of. For if inability for everything were other than inability for something else, then in the person there would be inabilities without end, and this is impossible. Also, death is the greatest of inabilities; because with it all actions are impossible. So if inability for everything were other than inability for something else, then some of the dead would have actions only absent due to the existence of [multiple] inabilities, and this necessitates that in one part there are two inabilities and two deaths. And if this were possible, it would be possible that one of them could rise to life, so that one part would be alive and dead simultaneously, and this is impossible. So when this is impossible, it is known that the statement of whoever said that inability for everything is other than inability for something else is impossible. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. An objector proposes that the absence of a thing and its opposite could be due to two distinct types of incapacity (ajz). The response begins by stating that there is a limit to what an incapable person can be incapable of—since he has no ability, he cannot be burdened with multiple distinct inabilities. If there were distinct inabilities for each thing, the person would have infinite inabilities, which is absurd. Next, Ash'ari uses death as an example: death is the greatest inability because it prevents all actions. If inabilities were distinct, then a dead person would have multiple inabilities, meaning multiple deaths in one body part, leading to the absurdity that one part could have both a death and a life simultaneously (if one inability were removed). This is impossible, therefore the premise that inabilities are distinct is false. The passage concludes decisively that the claim of multiple distinct inabilities is impossible.

Key Terms
عجز
عجزين
أعجاز
موت
حياة
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Incapacity for each action is distinct and separate from incapacity for another action.
Correction: Ash'ari demonstrates that this leads to infinite regress and contradictory states; therefore, incapacity is a single unified attribute.

Checkpoint
What absurdity does Ash'ari derive from the claim that inabilities are distinct?
A) A person could be both knowing and ignorant.
B) A dead person would have multiple deaths and could be partially alive.
C) God would be unjust.
D) There would be no difference between life and death.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari proves that inability is a unified attribute because distinct inabilities lead to impossible outcomes such as infinite regress and simultaneous opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused a sin by claiming 'I cannot help it'? This lesson shows that inability is a single attribute, not a separate excuse for each action. If you truly were incapable of that sin, would you not also be incapable of its opposite good? Examine your heart: where have you hidden behind false inability to avoid responsibility? Commit to one area you will stop claiming incapacity and instead seek Allah's help.
?
The dead person cannot act at all. Are there areas of your faith where you are 'spiritually dead'—prayers you neglect, duties you ignore? If inability cannot be partial, then your faith should be alive in all aspects. Reflect: do you treat some obligations as optional? What would it mean to revive that dead part? Make a concrete plan to bring it back to life.
?
Ash'ari uses logic to show that contradictory beliefs are impossible in reality. Do you hold any contradictory beliefs about Allah—for example, relying on Him but fearing creation more? This passage teaches that such contradictions cannot coexist. Pray for consistency in your tawheed and ask Allah to purify your heart from hidden shirk.

Answering the Mu'tazila on Quran 2:184
Ash'ari answers an objection about the pronoun reference in Quran 2:184.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's response to the Mu'tazila's grammatical objection regarding the pronoun in Quran 2:184, and why he prioritizes the interpretation of the Salaf over strict grammatical rules.

Core Idea
The Salaf's interpretation is the authority, not just grammar.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari addresses a specific objection from the Mu'tazila concerning the interpretation of Quran 2:184. The Mu'tazila argue that the pronoun in 'yutīqūnahu' must refer to fasting, but al-Ash'ari presents an alternative understanding rooted in early Muslim authority. Let's see how he defends his position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question and two interpretations
Read the passage to see the initial question and two possible meanings of 'yutīqūnahu' offered by al-Ash'ari.
Notice that al-Ash'ari presents both interpretations as possibilities without necessarily endorsing one yet.
2
Learn the Mu'tazila's grammatical objection
Identify the Mu'tazila's claim: the pronoun must refer to a previously mentioned word, which is 'fasting'.
This objection is based on a strict grammatical rule.
3
See al-Ash'ari's response
Examine al-Ash'ari's reply: his interpretation comes from the early Muslims (salaf), and grammarians are not a binding authority over the Companions and Followers.
The passage ends abruptly, but the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استشكال على مقارنة الاستطاعة للفعل
-٣١- مسألة
إن قال قائل : خبرونا عن قول الله -تعالى-: ﴿وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ ﴾ [البقرة : ١٨٤]؟
قيل له : يُحتمل أن يكون الله تعالى- أراد الذينَ يُطيقونَ الإطعام ويعجَزُونَ عَنِ الصيامِ : عليهِمُ الفِديةُ إِذا أَفْطَرُوا . ويُحتمل أن يكون أرادَ الذينَ يُطيقون الصيامَ إن تكلَّفُوه وأرادوه على قولِ مَنْ رجع بالهاءِ إلى مذكور تقدَّمَ على الصيام. فإن قالت المعتزلة : لا يجوز أن يُرجَع بها إلا إلى مذكور تقدم وهو الصيام.
قيل لهم : التأويلُ الذي تأولناه هو تأويل بعض المتقدمين، وليس النحويون حُجَّةً على الصحابة والتابعين وقد قرأها بعضُ
Objection regarding the comparison of capability to the act. -31- Issue: If someone says: Inform us about the saying of God Almighty: 'And upon those who are able (yutīqūnahu) a ransom' [Al-Baqarah 2:184]? It is said to him: It is possible that God Almighty intended those who are able to provide food and are incapable of fasting: upon them is the ransom if they break their fast. And it is possible that He intended those who are able to fast if they undertake it and desire it, according to the one who refers the pronoun (al-hā') to a previously mentioned referent before fasting. So if the Mu'tazila say: It is not permissible to refer it [the pronoun] except to a previously mentioned referent, which is fasting. It is said to them: The interpretation which we have interpreted is the interpretation of some of the predecessors, and the grammarians are not an authority against the Companions and the Followers. And indeed some of them recited it...
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer regarding the interpretation of Quran 2:184. The author first states the question: what does 'those who are able (yutīqūnahu)' mean? Two possible interpretations are given: either those able to feed the poor and incapable of fasting, or those able to fast with difficulty. The Mu'tazila then object that the pronoun in 'yutīqūnahu' must refer to the previously mentioned word 'fasting' (as-siyām), so the verse would mean 'those who are able to fast'. The author answers by pointing out that the interpretation he follows is from the early Muslims (salaf), and that grammarians are not a binding authority against the Companions and the Followers, who had deeper understanding. The passage ends abruptly, but the point is that the authority of early interpretation outweighs strict grammatical reasoning.

Correction: Rather, it prioritizes the interpretation of the Salaf as the proper method, not rejecting grammar outright.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Pronoun Reference
The author exposes the Mu'tazila's self-contradiction regarding pronoun reference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari author exposes a self-contradiction in the Mu'tazilite interpretation of a Quranic pronoun.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila contradict their own rule when interpreting a pronoun.

In this brief passage, the author presents a logical inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite reading of a Quranic verse. The Mu'tazila had a principle that a pronoun must refer to something explicitly mentioned earlier. Yet, in interpreting the pronoun in 'ātāhumā', they claim it refers to the polytheists among the offspring—who are not mentioned in the immediate context. This passage is a concise refutation that highlights the contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the concise style of the refutation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim and their principle
The Mu'tazila claim the pronoun refers to polytheists among the offspring. Their principle is that a pronoun must refer to a previously mentioned thing.
3
Spot the contradiction
The polytheists are not previously mentioned, so they violate their own rule. This is the core of the refutation.
Ask yourself why this contradiction is significant.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why exposing an internal contradiction is a powerful method of argumentation in theological debates.
This method is common in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زَعَمتِ المعتزلة أنَّ الهاء والميم [في : آتَاهُمَا ] لم يُرجَعْ بِهِما إلى ما تقدَّمَ ذِكرُه ، بل رُجِعَ بهما إلى المشركين من وَلَدِهما ، فَنَقَضُوا قولهم : إنَّ الهاء لا يُرجَعُ بها إلَّا إلى مذكورٍ قد تقدَّم ذكره .
The Mu'tazila claimed that the pronoun 'ha' and 'mim' [in 'ātāhumā'] are not referred back to what was previously mentioned, but rather are referred back to the polytheists among their offspring. So they contradicted their statement that the pronoun is only referred back to a mentioned thing that has been previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific Mu'tazilite interpretation of a Qur'anic verse. The author states the Mu'tazila's claim: the pronoun 'ha' and 'mim' in the word 'ātāhumā' (He gave them) from the verse (referring to Adam and Eve) does not refer back to the previously mentioned subjects (Adam and Eve), but rather to the polytheists among their children. The author then points out that this contradicts the Mu'tazila's own stated principle that a pronoun can only refer back to something that has been explicitly mentioned earlier. By violating that rule, they undermine their own position. The author does not elaborate further here; the passage is a concise exposure of inconsistency.

Interpreting 'Istita’ah' in the Hajj Verse
The author answers a question on the Hajj verse, defining ability as financial means.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari interpretation of 'istita'ah' in the Hajj verse as financial means, and how this supports the position that ability is simultaneous with the act.

Core Idea
The Hajj verse's 'ability' means financial provision, not bodily capacity.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari responds to an objection from the Mu'tazila about the Hajj verse. They argued that since the verse commands Hajj for those who are able, ability must precede the act. But the Ash'ari response is that the ability mentioned here is financial ability, not the bodily capacity to perform the act. This subtle distinction supports the broader Ash'ari doctrine that ability (istita'ah) accompanies the act, not precedes it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: a question (sa'alū) and a concise answer (al-jawāb).
2
Identify the key terms
The author defines 'sabīl' as al-māl (wealth), specifically al-zād (provisions) and al-rāḥila (mount). Note that 'istita'ah' here is financial.
3
Understand the argument from qiyās
The author claims that analogical evidence shows 'istita'ah' is with the act, and this supports their interpretation. Reflect on how this connects to the broader Ash'ari position.
This is a subtle point; the qiyās is not fully explained here.
4
Consider the opponents' view
The opponents allegedly interpret 'istita'ah' as bodily ability preceding the act. How does the author's financial interpretation invalidate their position?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سألُوا عن قول الله - تعالى -: ﴿وَلِلَّهِ عَلَى النَّاسِ حِجُ الْبَيْتِ مَنِ اسْتَطَاعَ إِلَيْهِ سَبِيلًا ﴾ [آل عمران : ۹۷] . فالجواب : أن الله -تعالى- أراد المال، وهو الزَّادُ والراحِلَةُ، ولم يُرِدِ استطاعةَ البدنِ التي في كونها كونُ مَقْدورِها . وقيام الدلالة من القياس على أنَّ الاستطاعة مع الفِعْلِ يُصحُحُ تأويلَنا ، ويُبْطِلُ تأويل مخالفينا .
And they asked about the saying of God - exalted -: 'And for God is the pilgrimage to the House for whoever is able to find a way to it' [Al 'Imran: 97]. The answer: that God - exalted - intended wealth, which is the provisions and the mount, and He did not intend bodily ability, whose existence is the existence of its capacity. And the evidence from analogy establishes that ability is with the act, which validates our interpretation and invalidates the interpretation of our opponents.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer format addressing the meaning of 'istita'ah' (ability) in the Hajj verse. Someone (likely the Mu'tazila) has asked about the verse. The author gives the answer: God intended by 'ability' here to mean financial means, specifically provisions (zād) and a mount (rāḥila) for travel, not bodily ability (istita'at al-badan) which would imply that the ability itself is the capacity (maqdūr) of the act. The author then asserts that analogical evidence (qiyās) shows that ability (istita'ah) occurs together with the act (ma'a al-fi'l), not before it. This interpretation (ta'wīl) of the author's school is thus supported by both the verse and reason, while the opponents' interpretation (that ability precedes the act) is invalidated. The argument is that the Hajj verse refers to financial ability, not the bodily capacity that they argue must precede the act; therefore their position collapses.

الزاد
الراحلة
القياس
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'istita'ah' in the Hajj verse refers to bodily ability preceding the act.
Correction: The author argues it means financial means (provisions and mount), and this aligns with the Ash'ari position that ability accompanies the act.

Checkpoint

D) Permission from the ruler

Key Takeaway
The ability in the Hajj verse is financial provision, not bodily capacity, which supports the Ash'ari doctrine that ability accompanies the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God's intended meaning of 'ability' in the Hajj verse is financial means and not your own bodily capacity, does that change how you view your own efforts in worship? Are there areas where you are relying on your own strength rather than seeking God's provision? What is one concrete step you can take this week to depend more on God's provision in your worship?

٣٢- مسألة وقد سألوا عن قول الله - تعالى -: ﴿وَلِلَّهِ عَلَى النَّاسِ حِجُ الْبَيْتِ مَنِ اسْتَطَاعَ إِلَيْهِ سَبِيلًا ﴾ [آل عمران : ۹۷] . فالجواب : أن الله -تعالى- أراد المال، وهو الزَّادُ والراحِلَةُ، ولم يُرِدِ استطاعةَ البدنِ التي في كونها كونُ مَقْدورِها . وقيام الدلالة من القياس على أنَّ الاستطاعة مع الفِعْلِ يُصحُحُ تأويلَنا ، ويُبْطِلُ تأويل مخالفينا .
32- Issue: They asked about the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And due to Allah from the people is the pilgrimage to the House - for whoever is able to find a way to it.' [Al-Imran 3:97] So the answer: That Allah the Exalted intended wealth, which is provision and mount, and He did not intend the bodily ability whose existence is the existence of its object of power. And the evidence from analogy that ability (istita'ah) is with the act validates our interpretation and invalidates the interpretation of our opponents.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The Mu'tazila ask about the Hajj verse (3:97) to argue that ability (istita'ah) must precede the act, because Hajj is only obligatory for those who have ability, implying ability exists before the act. Al-Ash'ari answers by distinguishing two types of ability: financial ability (provision and mount) which is a condition for obligation, and bodily ability which is simultaneous with the act. He argues that the verse refers to financial means, not bodily ability. He then invokes analogy (qiyas) as evidence that ability is with the act (i.e., simultaneous), which supports his interpretation and refutes the Mu'tazila who claim ability precedes.

The Hypocrites' False Oath About Ability
Al-Ash'ari explains the verse about hypocrites swearing they lacked ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's refutation of the Mu'tazilite interpretation of Quran 9:42 regarding ability (istita'ah).

Core Idea
The hypocrites' oath of inability was a lie; true ability was present.

In this passage, Imam Al-Ash'ari addresses a Mu'tazilite objection based on Quran 9:42, where hypocrites swear they lacked ability to join the Prophet in battle. The Mu'tazila used this to argue that ability precedes the act. Al-Ash'ari exposes the fallacy by showing the hypocrites were lying about their financial and material means, thus preserving the Ash'ari position that ability accompanies the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: a question followed by an answer.
The Arabic is concise; pay attention to the key terms 'استطاعة', 'الجدة', 'المال', 'الظهر'.
2
Identify the two possibilities posed
The objection presents two alternatives: either the hypocrites were truly unable and thus did not go out, or they were able but chose not to. Understand the Mu'tazilite trap.
3
Grasp Al-Ash'ari's answer
Al-Ash'ari explains that the hypocrites meant 'ability' as wealth and mounts, and they swore falsely that they lacked these. Allah declared them liars because they actually possessed them.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that ability must precede the act.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal implication
Consider how this interpretation defends the Ash'ari view that ability (istita'ah) does not precede the act but is simultaneous. The verse does not prove ability precedes; it merely exposes a lie.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣٣- مسألة إن قال قائل : ما مَعْنَى قولِ الله تعالى -: ﴿وَسَيَحْلِفُونَ بِاللَّهِ لَوِ اسْتَطَعْنَا لَخَرَجْنَا مَعَكُمْ ﴾ [التوبة : ٤٢] . هَلْ يَخلُو أَنْ يَكُونُوا : كانوا [غير] مستطيعين الخروج فلَمْ يخرُجوا ولو استَطاعُوا الخروج لم يَخْرُجُوا ؟ فالجواب: أنَّهم عَنَوْا بالاستطاعة الجدة والمال، وحَلَفُوا لرسولِ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنَّهم لا مال لهم ولا ظهر يَحمِلُونَ به مع نَبِيِّ الله صلى الله عليه فأكْذَبَهم الله في حلفهم ؛ لأنَّهم كانوا يجدون المال [والظهر] ولمْ تَكُن المناظرةُ بينَهم وبينَ رسولِ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم .
33- Issue: If someone says: What is the meaning of the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And they will swear by Allah, "If we were able, we would have gone out with you"' [At-Tawba 9:42]. Is it free from being that they were [not] able to go out and so did not go out, and if they were able they would not have gone out? So the answer: That they meant by ability (istita'ah) wealth and property, and they swore to the Messenger of Allah - may Allah bless him and grant him peace - that they had no wealth and no mount to carry them with the Prophet of Allah - may Allah bless him - so Allah declared them liars in their oath; because they did possess wealth [and mount], and the debate was not between them and the Messenger of Allah - may Allah bless him and grant him peace.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The opponent (likely Mu'tazila) asks about the verse (9:42) where hypocrites swear they lacked ability to go out for battle. The question presents two possibilities: either they were truly unable and thus did not go out, or they were able but chose not to go out. The Mu'tazila might use this to argue that ability (istita'ah) precedes the act (since they claim inability). Al-Ash'ari answers by explaining that the hypocrites meant by 'ability' wealth and property (provision and mount). They falsely swore they had no means, but Allah exposed their lie because they actually did have wealth and mounts. Thus, the verse does not establish that ability precedes the act; rather, the hypocrites were lying about their financial ability. The debate is not about the nature of ability but about their false excuse. This refutes the Mu'tazila's interpretation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hypocrites' claim of inability was genuine, proving that ability precedes the act.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the hypocrites were lying; they had wealth and mounts but pretended otherwise. Therefore, the verse does not prove that ability must precede the act.

Key Takeaway

?

?
If Allah exposed the hypocrites’ lie about their financial inability, what hidden excuses might He expose in your own life? This lesson demands that you examine your own claims of inability: are they sincere or self-deceptive? What is one area where you will honestly reassess your ability and take action?
?
The hypocrites swore by Allah while lying. How many times have you used Allah's name carelessly to cover up your own shortcomings? This verse confronts you with the seriousness of swearing falsely. Distinguishing Bodily Ability from Financial Means
Al-Ash'ari answers an objection based on Quran 9:42 regarding istita'a and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari defends his view on the relationship between ability (istita'a) and action by distinguishing between bodily capacity and financial resources, using Quran 9:42 as a case study.

Core Idea
The Quranic verse refers to money and mounts, not bodily ability.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari addresses an objection from the Quran that might seem to contradict his position. The objector argues that the verse shows ability can precede action, but Al-Ash'ari explains the context: the hypocrites were referring to financial resources, not physical ability. This distinction is crucial for understanding his theological stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector presents a dilemma from Quran 9:42: either the hypocrites were unable and thus didn't go out, or they were able but chose not to. This seems to imply that ability (istita'a) can exist without action.
The objector expects the verse to contradict Al-Ash'ari's view that ability is simultaneous with action.
2
Understand Al-Ash'ari's answer
Al-Ash'ari explains that 'istita'a' in the verse means financial means and mounts (al-jidda wal-mal). The hypocrites swore they lacked these, but Allah exposed them as liars because they actually had them.
The debate was about material provision, not about the timing of ability relative to action.
3
Connect to the larger issue
Al-Ash'ari clarifies that he does not deny that wealth can precede action; what he denies is that bodily ability can precede action. Thus, the verse does not support the objection.
This preserves his position that bodily ability (istita'at al-badan) is simultaneous with the action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنْ قالَ قائِلٌ : ما مَعْنَى قولِ الله تعالى -: ﴿وَسَيَحْلِفُونَ بِاللَّهِ لَوِ اسْتَطَعْنَا لَخَرَجْنَا مَعَكُمْ ﴾ [التوبة : ٤٢] . هَلْ يَخلُو أَنْ يَكُونُوا : كانوا [غير] مستطيعين الخروج فلَمْ يخرُجوا ولو استَطاعُوا الخروج لم يَخْرُجُوا ؟ فالجواب: أنَّهم عَنَوْا بالاستطاعة الجدة والمال، وحَلَفُوا لرسولِ الله -صلَّى الله عليه وسلَّم- أنَّهم لا مال لهم ولا ظهر يَحمِلُونَ به مع نَبِيِّ الله - صلى الله عليه - فأكْذَبَهم الله في حلفهم ؛ لأنَّهم كانوا يجدون المال [والظهر] ولمْ تَكُن المناظرةُ بينَهم وبينَ رسولِ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في أنَّ الاستطاعةَ مع الفِعلِ، أَوْ قَبْلَه . وإنَّما كانتِ المحاورة بينهم وبينه [صلى الله عليه وسلم] في الجِدَةِ والظَّهْرِ ، وهكذا ذكر أَهْلُ التَّفسير، ونقلةُ الأخبار، وحملة الآثار . وإذا كانَ هذَا هَكَذا ، فنَحْنُ لا ننكر تقدُّم المال للفِعْلِ ، وإِنَّما أنكرنا ثم تقدمَ استطاعةِ البَدنِ للفعل .
If a questioner says: What is the meaning of Allah's statement: 'And they will swear by Allah, if we had been able, we would have gone out with you' [At-Tawbah 9:42]? Does it not admit of only two possibilities: either they were unable to go out and so did not go out, or if they had been able to go out they would not have gone out? The answer: They meant by 'ability' means and wealth, and they swore to the Messenger of Allah - may Allah bless him and grant him peace - that they had no wealth or mounts to carry them with the Prophet of Allah - may Allah bless him - so Allah declared them liars in their oath; because they did possess wealth and mounts. And the discussion between them and the Messenger of Allah - may Allah bless him and grant him peace - was not about whether ability is with the action or before it. Rather, the exchange between them and him was about means and mounts. This is what the people of exegesis, the transmitters of reports, and the carriers of traditions have mentioned. If this is the case, we do not deny the precedence of wealth over action; we only deny the precedence of bodily ability over action.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector cites Quran 9:42, where hypocrites swear they would have gone out to battle if they had been able, and asks whether this verse implies that they were unable and thus did not go out, or that they were able but chose not to go out. Al-Ash'ari answers that the term 'istita'a' (ability) here refers to financial means and mounts, not bodily capacity. The hypocrites swore they lacked wealth and animals, but Allah exposed them as liars because they did possess these resources. The debate was not about whether ability (bodily) is before or with the action; it was about material provision. Al-Ash'ari then clarifies that he does not deny that wealth can precede action; what he denies is the precedence of bodily ability over action. Thus, the verse does not support the claim that ability precedes action in the relevant sense.
